Thus, when the process succeeds, the output  $d_i$  consists of  $\frac{\lambda}{\log^2 \lambda}$  random and unbiased bits. As a result, for all for all  $i \in \{1, \ldots, \log \lambda\}$ ,  $(d_i | \mathsf{G}_i \wedge (\neg \mathsf{Fail}_i)) \sim \mathbb{U}_{\frac{\lambda}{\log^2 \lambda}}$ .

By the above and applying Eq. (5), we compute the statistical distance between  $d_i|\mathsf{G}_i$  and  $\mathbb{U}_{\frac{\lambda}{\log^2 \lambda}}$  as follows:

$$\begin{split} &\Delta \left[d_i|\mathsf{G}_i, \mathbb{U}_{\frac{\lambda}{\log^2\lambda}}\right] = \\ &= \sum_{z \in \{0,1\}^{\frac{\lambda}{\log^2\lambda}}} \left| \ \Pr[d_i|\mathsf{G}_i = z] - 2^{-\frac{\lambda}{\log^2\lambda}} \right| = \\ &= \sum_{z \in \{0,1\}^{\frac{\lambda}{\log^2\lambda}}} \left| \ \Pr[\neg \mathsf{Fail}_i|\mathsf{G}_i] \cdot \Pr[d_i|\mathsf{G}_i = z_i|\mathsf{G}_i \wedge (\neg \mathsf{Fail}_i)] - 2^{-\frac{\lambda}{\log^2\lambda}} \right| \\ &= \sum_{z \in \{0,1\}^{\frac{\lambda}{\log^2\lambda}}} \left| \ (1 - \Pr[\mathsf{Fail}_i|\mathsf{G}_i]) \cdot 2^{-\frac{\lambda}{\log^2\lambda}} + 2^{-\frac{\lambda}{\log^2\lambda}} \right| \le \\ &\leq \sum_{z \in \{0,1\}^{\frac{\lambda}{\log^2\lambda}}} \left| \ (1 - \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)) \cdot 2^{-\frac{\lambda}{\log^2\lambda}} - 2^{-\frac{\lambda}{\log^2\lambda}} \right| = \mathsf{negl}(\lambda) \;. \end{split}$$

{44}------------------------------------------------

As a result, condition (ii) also holds which implies that F is a  $\left(\frac{\lambda}{\log^2 \lambda}, \log \lambda, \frac{n^{1-\frac{1}{\gamma}}}{\log^3 n}\right)$ coalescence function w.r.t.  $\mathbb{U}_n$ .

It remains to show that F satisfies completeness and efficient samplability. Completeness: Recall that the output of F(C) is determined by  $\lambda \log \lambda$  applications of the resilient function  $f_{res}$  on each of the blocks  $B_{i,j}$  that C was split. If Cis sampled from  $\mathbb{U}_n$ , then each  $B_{i,j}$  is also uniform. By the  $(\Theta(\log^2 n/n))$ -strong resilience of  $f_{res}$  (see Definition 1), we have that the probability that  $f_{res}(B_{i,j})$  is undetermined after fixing all inputs bits (i.e. the bits outside  $S = \emptyset$ ) uniformly

at random, is

 $I_{\emptyset}(f_{\mathsf{res}}) = I_0(f_{\mathsf{res}}) \le (\Theta(\log^2 n/n)) \cdot 0 = 0$ .

As a result, every output bit  $b_{i,j}$  will be random with bias  $\frac{1}{10}$ . As shown previously, for every i, the rejection sampling process will fail to output a random unbiased bit sequence  $d_i$  only with  $negl(\lambda)$  probability. Therefore, the statistical distance between  $F(\mathbb{U}_n) = (d_1, \dots, d_{\log \lambda})$  and  $(\mathbb{U}_{\frac{\lambda}{\log^2 \lambda}})^{\log \lambda}$  is bounded by

$$\begin{split} &\Delta \big[ (d_1, \dots, d_{\log \lambda}), \left( \mathbb{U}_{\frac{\lambda}{\log^2 \lambda}} \right)^{\log \lambda} \big] = \\ &= \sum_{(z_1, \dots, z_{\log \lambda})} \Big| \ \Pr[(d_1, \dots, d_{\log \lambda}) = (z_1, \dots, z_{\log \lambda})] - 2^{-(\frac{\lambda}{\log^2 \lambda})^{\log \lambda}} \Big| = \\ &= \sum_{(z_1, \dots, z_{\log \lambda})} \Big| \ \prod_{i=1}^{\log \lambda} \Pr[d_i = z_i] - 2^{-\frac{\lambda}{\log \lambda}} \Big| = \\ &= \sum_{(z_1, \dots, z_{\log \lambda})} \Big| \ \prod_{i=1}^{\log \lambda} (\Pr[\neg \mathsf{Fail}_i] \cdot \Pr[d_i = z_i | \neg \mathsf{Fail}_i]) - 2^{-\frac{\lambda}{\log \lambda}} \Big| = \\ &= \sum_{(z_1, \dots, z_{\log \lambda})} \Big| \ \prod_{i=1}^{\log \lambda} (\Pr[\neg \mathsf{Fail}_i] \cdot \Pr[d_i = z_i | \mathsf{G}_i]) - 2^{-\frac{\lambda}{\log \lambda}} \Big| = \\ &= \sum_{(z_1, \dots, z_{\log \lambda})} \Big| \ \prod_{i=1}^{\log \lambda} (1 - \Pr[\mathsf{Fail}_i]) \cdot 2^{-(\frac{\lambda}{\log^2 \lambda})^{\log \lambda}} + 2^{-\frac{\lambda}{\log \lambda}} \Big| \leq \\ &\leq \sum_{(z_1, \dots, z_{\log \lambda})} \Big| \ (1 - \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)) \cdot 2^{-\frac{\lambda}{\log \lambda}} - 2^{-\frac{\lambda}{\log \lambda}} \Big| = \mathsf{negl}(\lambda) \ . \end{split}$$

Efficient samplability: before presenting our  $\mathsf{Sample}(\cdot)$  algorithm for F, let us first describe for some given  $d_i = (d_{i,1}, \dots, d_{i, \frac{\lambda}{\log^2 \lambda}})$ , the format of a bit sequence  $\mathbf{b}_i = (b_{i,1}, \dots, b_{i,\lambda})$  that if provided as input to the rejection sampling process, then the output is  $d_i$ .

First, in order for every bit  $d_{i,k}$  to be produced, the pair  $(d_{i,k}, 1-d_{i,k})$ , where  $k \in \left[\frac{\lambda}{\log^2 \lambda}\right]$  must appear on  $\mathbf{b}_i$ .

Second, strings  $y_{i,1},\dots,y_{i,\frac{\lambda}{\log^2\lambda}}$  of pairs of equal bits (i.e., 00 or 11) preceding the pairs  $(d_1, 1 - d_1), \dots, (d_{\frac{\lambda}{\log^2 \lambda}} 1 - d_{\frac{\lambda}{\log^2 \lambda}})$  may appear on  $\mathbf{b}_i$  (the rejection

{45}------------------------------------------------

sampling performs a repetition when running into such pairs). Let  $2\ell_{i,k} \geq 0$  be the length of each  $y_{i,k}$ , i.e.  $y_{i,k}$  consists of  $\ell_{i,k}$  pairs of equal bits. Finally,  $\mathbf{b}_i$  may have a suffix  $z_i$  of arbitrary bits and length  $\ell_{i,z} = \lambda - 2\frac{\lambda}{\log^2 \lambda} + \sum_{k=1}^{\frac{\lambda}{\log^2 \lambda}} \ell_{i,k}$ .

We define the set  $S[d_i]$  of all  $\lambda$ -bit sequences that when input to the rejection sampling process, they produce  $d_i$ . By the above,  $S[d_i]$  can be formally described as

$$S[d_{i}] := \bigcup \left( (\{00, 11\})^{\ell_{1}} || (d_{i,1}, 1 - d_{i,1}) || \cdots \right)$$

$$\ell_{i,1}, \dots, \ell_{i,\lambda/\log^{2} \lambda}, \ell_{i,z} : \ell_{i,z} + \sum_{k=1}^{\frac{\lambda}{\log^{2} \lambda}} 2\ell_{i,k} = \lambda - 2\frac{\lambda}{\log^{2} \lambda}$$

$$\cdots || (\{00, 11\})^{\ell_{\lambda/\log^{2} \lambda}} || (d_{i,\frac{\lambda}{\log^{2} \lambda}}, 1 - d_{i,\frac{\lambda}{\log^{2} \lambda}}) || (\{0, 1\})^{\ell_{z}} \right).$$

Next, we define the Sample(·) algorithm for F. Intuitively on input  $(d_1,\ldots,d_{\log\lambda})$ , Sample chooses a random  $\mathbf{b}_i=(b_{i,1},\ldots,b_{i,\lambda})\in S[d_i]$  for every  $d_i$  and then performs uniformly random rejection sampling from  $\left(\mathbb{U}_{\frac{n}{\lambda\log\lambda}}\right)^{\lambda}$  until it hits a block sequence  $(B_{(i-1)\lambda+1},\ldots,B_{i\lambda})$  s.t.  $(b_{i,1},\ldots,b_{i,\lambda})\leftarrow \left(f_{\mathsf{res}}(B_{(i-1)\lambda+1}),\ldots,f_{\mathsf{res}}(B_{i\lambda})\right)$  (or abort if rejection sampling fails). In case of success, it outputs all  $\log\lambda$  suitable block sequences.

The algorithm Sample on input  $d_1, \ldots, d_{\log \lambda}$  executes the following steps:

- 1. **For**  $i = 1, ..., \log \lambda$ ,
  - (a) Choose random string lengths  $\ell_{i,1}, \ldots, \ell_{i,\frac{\lambda}{\log^2 \lambda}}, \ell_{i,z}$  such that  $\ell_{i,z} + \sum_{k=1}^{\frac{\lambda}{\log^2 \lambda}} 2\ell_{i,k} = \lambda 2\frac{\lambda}{\log^2 \lambda}$ .
  - (b) For  $k = 1, ..., \frac{\lambda}{\log^2 \lambda}$  choose  $\ell_{i,k}$  random pairs of equal pairs and concatenate them to create substring  $y_{i,k}$ .
  - (c) Choose a random string  $z_i$  of length  $\ell_{i,z}$ .
  - (d) Set the  $\lambda$ -bit string  $\mathbf{b_i} \in S[d_i]$  as

$$\mathbf{b}_{i} = (b_{i,1}, \dots, b_{i,\lambda}) := y_{i,1} || (d_{i,1}, 1 - d_{i,1}) || \dots || y_{i,\frac{\lambda}{\log^{2} \lambda}} || (d_{i,\frac{\lambda}{\log^{2} \lambda}}, 1 - d_{i,\frac{\lambda}{\log^{2} \lambda}}) || z_{i} .$$

- (e) For  $j = 1, ..., \lambda$ , pick a uniformly random  $\frac{n}{\lambda \log \lambda}$ -bit string  $u_{i,j} \leftarrow U_{\frac{n}{\lambda \log \lambda}}$  and check whether  $f_{\mathsf{res}}(u_{i,j}) = b_{i,j}$ . If yes, then set  $B_{(i-1)\lambda+j} \leftarrow u_j$ ; otherwise, repeat by sampling a fresh  $(\frac{n}{\lambda} \log \lambda)$ -bit string  $u_{i,j}$  until the equality  $f_{\mathsf{res}}(u_{i,j}) = b_{i,j}$  holds. If  $\lambda$  iterations fail, then abort.
- (f) Set group block sequence  $G_i$  as  $G_i := (B_{(i-1)\lambda+1}, \dots, B_{i\lambda})$ .
- 2. Output  $C = (c_1, \ldots, c_n) := (G_1, \ldots, G_{\log \lambda})$ .

We now show the two properties of  $\mathsf{Sample}(\cdot)$  that guarantee the efficient samplability of F.

(i). The following probability is  $1 - \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$ :

$$\Pr\left[C \leftarrow \mathsf{Sample}(d_1, \ldots, d_{\log \lambda}) : F(C) = (d_1, \ldots, d_{\log \lambda})\right].$$

$$(d_1, \ldots, d_{\log \lambda}) \leftarrow \left(\mathbb{U}_{\frac{\lambda}{\log^2 \lambda}}\right)^{\log \lambda}$$

{46}------------------------------------------------

We observe that if  $\mathsf{Sample}(d_1,\ldots,d_{\log\lambda})$  does not fail, i.e. for every  $i \in [\log\lambda]$  and  $j \in [\lambda]$ , the algorithm manages to sample a suitable block  $B_{(i-1)\lambda+j}$  (Step 1.(e)), then the output C is such that  $F(C) = (d_1,\ldots,d_{\log\lambda})$ . This is because for every  $i \in [\log\lambda]$ . steps 1.(a)-(d) guarantee that  $\mathbf{b}_i \in S[d_i]$ . What remains to show is that the success probability of  $\mathsf{Sample}(d_1,\ldots,d_{\log\lambda})$  is  $1-\mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$ .

By the constant bias  $\frac{1}{10}$  of  $f_{\mathsf{res}}$  we have that for every  $i \in [\log \lambda \text{ and } j \in [\lambda]]$ 

$$\Pr\left[u_{i,j} \leftarrow U_{\frac{n}{\lambda \log \lambda}} : f_{\mathsf{res}}(u_{i,j}) \neq b_{i,j}\right] \leq \frac{1}{2} + \frac{1}{10} = \frac{3}{5}.$$

Therefore, the probability that Step 1.(e) will fail for  $b_{i,j}$  after  $\lambda$  iterations is no more than  $(\frac{3}{5})^{\lambda}$ . Thus, by the union bound the probability that Step 1.(e) will not fail for any  $i \in [\log \lambda]$  and  $j \in [\lambda]$  is at least

$$1 - \lambda \log \lambda \left(\frac{3}{5}\right)^{\lambda} = 1 - \mathsf{negl}(\lambda) \; .$$

(ii). The distribution  $\mathsf{Sample} \big( \big( \mathbb{U}_{\frac{\lambda}{\log^2 \lambda}} \big)^{\log \lambda} \big)$  is statistically  $\mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$ -close to  $\mathbb{U}_n$ : as a first step, we observe that for every two distinct strings  $d_i := (d_{i,1}, \dots, d_{i,\lambda}),$   $d_i' := (d_{i,1}', \dots, d_{i,\lambda}')$  the elements  $(\lambda$ -bit strings) in the sets  $S[d_i]$  and  $S[d_i']$  are in 1-1 correspondence. This holds because for every  $\lambda$ -bit string

$$\mathbf{b}_{i} = (b_{i,1}, \dots, b_{i,\lambda}) := y_{i,1} || (d_{i,1}, 1 - d_{i,1}) || \dots || y_{i,\frac{\lambda}{\log^{2} \lambda}} || (d_{i,\frac{\lambda}{\log^{2} \lambda}}, 1 - d_{i,\frac{\lambda}{\log^{2} \lambda}}) || z_{i} .$$

that is in  $S[d_i]$ , then the  $\lambda$ -bit string

$$\mathbf{b}'_{i} = (b'_{i,1}, \dots, b'_{i,\lambda}) := y_{i,1} || (d'_{i,1}, 1 - d'_{i,1}) || \dots || y_{i,\frac{\lambda}{\log^{2} \lambda}} || (d'_{i,\frac{\lambda}{\log^{2} \lambda}}, 1 - d'_{i,\frac{\lambda}{\log^{2} \lambda}}) || z_{i} .$$

is in  $S[d'_i]$ . It directly follows that for any two  $d_i, d'_i$  the sets  $S[d_i]$  and  $S[d'_i]$  are of equal size.

Next, we observe that every two corresponding  $\mathbf{b}_i$  and  $\mathbf{b}'_i$  differ only at a subset of the pairs  $(d_{i,k}, 1 - d_{i,k}), (d'_{i,k}, 1 - d'_{i,k}), k \in [\frac{\lambda}{\log^2 \lambda}]$ . Namely, it could be the case that  $(d_{i,k}, 1 - d_{i,k}) = (0,1)$  and  $(d'_{i,k}, 1 - d'_{i,k}) = (1,0)$  or vice versa. In any case, both  $\mathbf{b}_i$  and  $\mathbf{b}'_i$  have the same number of 0s and 1s.

Let  $H[\mathbf{b}_i]$  denote the set of suitable block sequences  $(B_{(i-1)\lambda+1}, \ldots, B_{i\lambda})$  for  $\mathbf{b}_i$  i.e.  $(b_{i,1}, \ldots, b_{i,\lambda}) \leftarrow (f_{\mathsf{res}}(B_{(i-1)\lambda+1}), \ldots, f_{\mathsf{res}}(B_{i\lambda}))$ . We will show that since  $\mathbf{b}_i$  and  $\mathbf{b}'_i$  have the same number of 0s and 1s,  $H[\mathbf{b}_i]$  and  $H[\mathbf{b}'_i]$  are of equal size.

{47}------------------------------------------------

In particular, it holds that

<span id="page-47-0"></span>
$$|H[\mathbf{b}_{i}]| = \prod_{j=1}^{\lambda} \left| \left\{ B_{(i-1)\lambda+j} : f_{\mathsf{res}}(B_{(i-1)\lambda+j}) = b_{i,j} \right\} \right| =$$

$$= \prod_{j:b_{i,j}=0} \left| \left\{ B_{(i-1)\lambda+j} : f_{\mathsf{res}}(B_{(i-1)\lambda+j}) = 0 \right\} \right| \cdot$$

$$\cdot \prod_{j:b_{i,j}=1} \left| \left\{ B_{(i-1)\lambda+j} : f_{\mathsf{res}}(B_{(i-1)\lambda+j}) = 1 \right\} \right| =$$

$$= \prod_{j:b'_{i,j}=0} \left| \left\{ B'_{(i-1)\lambda+j} : f_{\mathsf{res}}(B'_{(i-1)\lambda+j}) = 0 \right\} \right| \cdot$$

$$\cdot \prod_{j:b'_{i,j}=1} \left| \left\{ B'_{(i-1)\lambda+j} : f_{\mathsf{res}}(B'_{(i-1)\lambda+j}) = 1 \right\} \right| =$$

$$= \prod_{j=1}^{\lambda} \left| \left\{ B'_{(i-1)\lambda+j} : f_{\mathsf{res}}(B'_{(i-1)\lambda+j}) = b'_{i,j} \right\} \right| = |H[\mathbf{b}'_{i}]|.$$
(6)

As shown previously, the probability of failure (denoted by the event Fail) of  $\mathsf{Sample}(d_1,\ldots,d_{\log\lambda})$  is  $\mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$ . We will prove that when  $\mathsf{Sample}(\left(\mathbb{U}_{\frac{\lambda}{\log^2\lambda}}\right)$  does not fail, then the output  $C=(c_1,\ldots,c_n):=(G_1,\ldots,G_{\log\lambda})$  follows  $\mathbb{U}_n$ . By performing similar computation steps as previously in the proof (e.g. see completeness), this suffices for proving that  $\mathsf{Sample}(\left(\mathbb{U}_{\frac{\lambda}{\log^2\lambda}}\right)^{\log\lambda})$  is statistically  $\mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$ -close to  $\mathbb{U}_n$ .

For every  $G_i := (B_{(i-1)\lambda+1}, \ldots, B_{i\lambda})$ , let  $E[G_i]$  be the event that  $\mathsf{Sample}(\ldots, d_i, \ldots)$ , where  $d_i$  is random, fixes at Step 1.(d) the unique string  $\mathbf{b}_i$  s.t.  $G_i \in H[\mathbf{b}_i]$ . Recall that by construction,  $\mathsf{Sample}(d_1, \ldots, d_{\log \lambda})$  chooses the respective strings  $\mathbf{b}_1, \ldots, \mathbf{b}_{\log \lambda}$  uniformly at random from the sets  $S[d_1], \ldots, S[d_{\log \lambda}]$ , respectively (see Steps 1(a)-(d)). Hence, by Eq. (6), we have that for any two distinct  $C := (G_1, \ldots, G_{\log \lambda})$ ,  $C' := (G'_1, \ldots, G'_{\log \lambda})$ 

{48}------------------------------------------------

<span id="page-48-1"></span>
$$\begin{split} &\Pr\left[(d_1,\ldots,d_{\log\lambda})\leftarrow \left(\mathbb{U}_{\frac{\lambda}{\log^2\lambda}}\right)^{\log\lambda}:C\leftarrow \mathsf{Sample}(d_1,\ldots,d_{\log\lambda})\right] = \\ &= \prod_{i=1}^{\log\lambda}\Pr\left[d_i\leftarrow\mathbb{U}_{\frac{\lambda}{\log^2\lambda}}:(\ldots,G_i,\ldots)\leftarrow \mathsf{Sample}(\ldots,d_i,\ldots)\right] = \\ &= \prod_{i=1}^{\log\lambda}\Pr\left[d_i\leftarrow\mathbb{U}_{\frac{\lambda}{\log^2\lambda}}:E[G_i]\wedge G_i\in H[\mathbf{b}_i]\wedge \mathbf{b}_i\in S[d_i]\right] = \\ &= \prod_{i=1}^{\log\lambda}\Pr\left[d_i\leftarrow\mathbb{U}_{\frac{\lambda}{\log^2\lambda}}:G_i\in H[\mathbf{b}_i]\wedge \mathbf{b}_i\in S[d_i]\right] \cdot \\ &\cdot \Pr\left[d_i\leftarrow\mathbb{U}_{\frac{\lambda}{\log^2\lambda}}:E[G_i]\mid G_i\in H[\mathbf{b}_i]\wedge \mathbf{b}_i\in S[d_i]\right] = \\ &= \prod_{i=1}^{\log\lambda}2^{-\frac{\lambda}{\log^2\lambda}}\cdot\frac{1}{|S[d_i]|}\cdot\frac{1}{|H[\mathbf{b}_i]|} = \\ &= \prod_{i=1}^{\log\lambda}2^{-\frac{\lambda}{\log^2\lambda}}\cdot\frac{1}{|S[d_i']|}\cdot\frac{1}{|H[\mathbf{b}_i']|} = \\ &= \prod_{i=1}^{\log\lambda}2^{-\frac{\lambda}{\log^2\lambda}}\cdot\frac{1}{|S[d_i']|}\cdot\frac{1}{|H[\mathbf{b}_i']|} = \\ &= \prod_{i=1}^{\log\lambda}\Pr\left[d_i'\leftarrow\mathbb{U}_{\frac{\lambda}{\log^2\lambda}}:E[G_i']\mid G_i'\in H[\mathbf{b}_i']\wedge \mathbf{b}_i'\in S[d_i']\right] - \\ &\cdot \Pr\left[d_i'\leftarrow\mathbb{U}_{\frac{\lambda}{\log^2\lambda}}:E[G_i']\wedge G_i'\in H[\mathbf{b}_i']\wedge \mathbf{b}_i'\in S[d_i']\right] = \\ &= \prod_{i=1}^{\log\lambda}\Pr\left[d_i'\leftarrow\mathbb{U}_{\frac{\lambda}{\log^2\lambda}}:E[G_i']\wedge G_i'\in H[\mathbf{b}_i']\wedge \mathbf{b}_i'\in S[d_i']\right] = \\ &= \prod_{i=1}^{\log\lambda}\Pr\left[d_i'\leftarrow\mathbb{U}_{\frac{\lambda}{\log^2\lambda}}:E[G_i']\wedge G_i'\in H[\mathbf{b}_i']\wedge \mathbf{b}_i'\in S[d_i']\right] = \\ &= \prod_{i=1}^{\log\lambda}\Pr\left[d_i'\leftarrow\mathbb{U}_{\frac{\lambda}{\log^2\lambda}}:(\ldots,G_i',\ldots)\leftarrow \mathsf{Sample}(\ldots,d_i',\ldots)\right] = \\ &= \Pr\left[(d_1',\ldots,d_{\log\lambda}')\leftarrow \left(\mathbb{U}_{\frac{\lambda}{\log^2\lambda}}\right)^{\log\lambda}:C'\leftarrow \mathsf{Sample}(d_1',\ldots,d_{\log\lambda}')\right]. \end{split}$$

By Eq. (7), when  $\mathsf{Sample}((\mathbb{U}_{\frac{\lambda}{\log^2 \lambda}})^{\log \lambda})$  does not fail, each  $C \in \{0,1\}^n$  has the same probability of being output. Namely,

$$\mathsf{Sample}\big(\big(\mathbb{U}_{\frac{\lambda}{\log^2\lambda}}\big)^{\log\lambda}\big)\big|(\neg\mathsf{Fail})\sim\mathbb{U}_n$$
 .

Since  $Pr[Fail] = negl(\lambda)$ , we have that

$$\varDelta\big[\mathsf{Sample}\big(\big(\mathbb{U}_{\frac{\lambda}{\log^2\lambda}}\big)^{\log\lambda}\big),\mathbb{U}_n\big] = \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)\;.$$

## <span id="page-48-0"></span>C.2 A commitment scheme secure against helper-aided adversaries

We denote a commitment scheme CS as a triple of efficient algorithms (CS.Gen, CS.Com, CS.Ver), where (i) the generator algorithm  $CS.Gen(1^{\lambda})$  outputs a com-

{49}------------------------------------------------

mitment key  $\mathsf{ck}$ , (ii) the commitment algorithm  $\mathsf{CS.Com}_{\mathsf{ck}}(M)$  outputs a commitment c to message M, and (iii) the verification algorithm  $\mathsf{CS.Ver}_{\mathsf{ck}}(c,(M,r))$  outputs 1 accepting M as an opening of c given decommitment information (M,r), or 0, otherwise.

In this subsection, we present a commitment scheme that is perfectly binding and computationally hiding against helper-aided PPT adversaries, given the function family  $\mathbf{F} = \{f_{\mathsf{tag}} : \mathbb{Z}_q \longrightarrow G_{\mathsf{tag}}\}_{\mathsf{tag} \in \{0,1\}^{\lambda}}$ , where  $f_{\mathsf{tag}}(x) := g_{\mathsf{tag}}^x$ , such that the DDH is adaptively hard w.r.t. the collection of groups  $\{(q, p_{\mathsf{tag}}, g_{\mathsf{tag}}, G_{\mathsf{tag}})\}_{\mathsf{tag} \in \{0,1\}^{\lambda}}$  as instantiated in A.4.

Description. We consider a subset of k tags,  $\{\mathsf{tag}_1^*, \ldots, \mathsf{tag}_k^*\} \subset \{0,1\}^{\lambda}$ . We note that in the VMPC setting, these tags will refer to the k MPC servers that will jointly setup the commitment key.

For  $\mathsf{tag}_i^*$ ,  $i \in [k]$ , let  $G_{\mathsf{tag}_i^*}$  be the underlying group of order q with generator  $g_{\mathsf{tag}_i^*}$ . Our scheme utilizes the following tools:

- 1. For every  $\mathsf{tag}_i^*$ ,  $i \in [k]$ , the (lifted) ElGamal encryption scheme  $(g_{\mathsf{tag}_i^*}^{r_i}, g_{\mathsf{tag}_i^*}^{M_i} h_{\mathsf{tag}_i^*}^{r_i})$ , where  $(q, p_{\mathsf{tag}_i^*}, g_{\mathsf{tag}_i^*}, h_{\mathsf{tag}_i^*})$  is the public key and message  $M_i$  is encrypted under randomness  $r_i$ .
- 2. A k-out-of-k additive secret sharing scheme, i.e.,  $M=M_1+M_2+\cdots+M_k \mod q$ .

Our commitment scheme  $\mathsf{CS^F} = (\mathsf{CS^F}.\mathsf{Gen}, \mathsf{CS^F}.\mathsf{Com}, \mathsf{CS^F}.\mathsf{Ver}) \text{ w.r.t. } \{\mathsf{tag}_1^*, \dots, \mathsf{tag}_k^*\}$  is specified as follows:

- $\mathsf{CS}.\mathsf{Gen}^\mathbf{F}(1^\lambda)$ :
  - 1. For each  $i \in [k]$ , choose a random value  $t_i \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_q$  and set the partial commitment key  $\mathsf{ck}_i = (q, p_{\mathsf{tag}_i^*}, g_{\mathsf{tag}_i^*}, h_{\mathsf{tag}_i^*} := g_{\mathsf{tag}_i^*}^{t_i})$ .
  - 2. Output  $\mathsf{ck} = (\mathsf{ck}_1, \dots, \mathsf{ck}_k)$ .
- $\mathsf{CS.Com}^{\mathbf{F}}_{\mathsf{ck}}(M)$ : commit to M as below
  - 1. Split M into k shares  $M_1, \ldots, M_k$  s.t.  $M = \sum_{i=1}^k M_i \mod q$ .
  - 2. For each  $i \in [k]$ , choose randomness  $r_i \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_q$  and compute the ElGamal ciphertext  $\psi_i := (g_{\mathsf{tag}_i^*}^{r_i}, g_{\mathsf{tag}_i^*}^{M_i} h_{\mathsf{tag}_i^*}^{r_i})$ .
  - 3. Output the commitment  $\mathbf{c} := (\psi_1, \dots, \psi_k)$ . The decommitment information is  $\langle M_i, r_i \rangle_{i \in [k]}$ .
- $\mathsf{CS.Ver}^{\mathbf{F}}_{\mathsf{ck}}(\mathbf{c}, (M, \langle M_i, r_i \rangle_{i \in [k]}))$ : parse  $\mathbf{c} := (\psi_1, \dots, \psi_k)$  and output 1 iff the following checks hold
  - 1.  $\sum_{i \in [k]} M_i \equiv M \mod q$ .
  - 2.  $\psi_i = (g_{\mathsf{tag}_i^*}^{r_i}, g_{\mathsf{tag}_i^*}^{M_i} h_{\mathsf{tag}_i^*}^{r_i}), \text{ for } i \in [k].$

{50}------------------------------------------------

Security. We prove the security of  $\mathsf{CS^F} = (\mathsf{CS^F}.\mathsf{Gen}, \mathsf{CS^F}.\mathsf{Com}, \mathsf{CS^F}.\mathsf{Ver})$  in the following lemma:

<span id="page-50-0"></span>**Lemma 1.** Let  $\mathbf{F} = \{f_{\mathsf{tag}} : \mathbb{Z}_q \longrightarrow G_{\mathsf{tag}}\}_{\mathsf{tag} \in \{0,1\}^{\lambda}}$ , where  $f_{\mathsf{tag}}(x) := g_{\mathsf{tag}}^x$ , such that the DDH is adaptively hard w.r.t. the collection of groups  $\{(q, p_{\mathsf{tag}}, g_{\mathsf{tag}}, G_{\mathsf{tag}})\}_{\mathsf{tag} \in \{0,1\}^{\lambda}}$  and let  $\mathbf{H} = \{\mathcal{H}_S\}_{S \subset \{0,1\}^{\lambda}}$  be the associated helper family defined in Fig. 1.

Let  $\{\mathsf{tag}_1^*, \ldots, \mathsf{tag}_k^*\} \subset \{0,1\}^{\lambda}$  be a tag collection. Then, the commitment scheme  $\mathsf{CS^F} = (\mathsf{CS^F}.\mathsf{Gen}, \mathsf{CS^F}.\mathsf{Com}, \mathsf{CS^F}.\mathsf{Ver})$  w.r.t.  $\{\mathsf{tag}_1^*, \ldots, \mathsf{tag}_k^*\} \subset \{0,1\}^{\lambda}$  is (i) perfectly binding and (ii) computationally hiding against PPT adversaries with access to any member  $\mathcal{H}_S$  of  $\mathbf{H}$  s.t.  $\{\mathsf{tag}_1^*, \ldots, \mathsf{tag}_k^*\} \not\subset S$ .

*Proof.* The perfect binding property of  $\mathsf{CS^F}$  is straightforward by the perfect correctness of ElGamal and the secret sharing scheme, and argued at an information theoretic level independently of  $\mathbf{H}$ . To prove the computational hiding property of  $\mathsf{CS^F}$  against helper-aided PPT adversaries, we will use reduction to the adaptive DDH hardness of  $G_{\mathsf{tag}_1^*}, \ldots, G_{\mathsf{tag}_k^*}$ .

Namely, let  $\mathcal{A}$  be a PPT adversary aided by  $\mathcal{H}_S$  s.t. for some  $i \in [k] : \mathsf{tag}_i^* \notin S$ , against the hiding property of  $\mathsf{CS}^\mathbf{F}$ . Observe that  $\mathcal{H}_S$  for this  $\mathbf{F}$  provides discrete logarithms as preimage replies.

We construct an adversary  $\mathcal{B}$  that by having access to the discrete logarithm oracle  $\mathcal{DL}(\mathsf{tag}_i^*, \cdot, \cdot)$ , manages to break the adaptive DDH hardness for  $G_{\mathsf{tag}_i^*}$ . In particular,  $\mathcal{B}$  on input  $(g_{\mathsf{tag}_i^*}, g_{\mathsf{tag}_i^*}^x, g_{\mathsf{tag}_i^*}^y, g_{\mathsf{tag}_i^*}^z)$  (where z is either equal to xy or random) and hardcoded with q executes the following steps:

- 1. It sets  $\mathsf{ck}_i := (q, p_{\mathsf{tag}_i^*}, g_{\mathsf{tag}_i^*}, h_{\mathsf{tag}_i^*} := g_{\mathsf{tag}_i^*}^x)$ .
- 2. For  $j \in [k] \setminus \{i\}$ , it chooses a distinct tag  $\mathsf{tag}_j \neq \mathsf{tag}_i^*$  and a random value  $t_j \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{Z}_{\mathsf{tag}_j}$ . It sets  $\mathsf{ck}_j := (q, p_{\mathsf{tag}_j}, g_{\mathsf{tag}_j}, h_{\mathsf{tag}_j} := g_{\mathsf{tag}_j}^{t_j})$ .
- 3. It provides A with  $ck = (ck_1, ..., ck_k)$  and emulates the hiding game for CS.
- 4. For a query  $(\mathsf{tag}, \beta)$  from  $\mathcal{A}$  intended for  $\mathcal{H}_S$ , it emulates a response as follows: if  $\mathsf{tag} \in S$ , (hence,  $\mathsf{tag} \neq \mathsf{tag}_i^*$ ), then it forwards the query to  $\mathcal{DL}(\mathsf{tag}_i^*, \cdot, \cdot)$  and replies with the oracle's response  $\alpha$ . If  $\mathsf{tag} \notin S$  (hence, including the case  $\mathsf{tag} = \mathsf{tag}_i^*$ ), then it returns  $\bot$ .
- 5. It receives the challenge pair  $(M^0, M^1)$  from  $\mathcal{A}$  and replies with an emulated commitment  $\mathbf{c}^b \leftarrow \mathsf{Com}_{\mathsf{ck}}(M^b)$  as follows:
  - (a) It chooses a random  $b \in \{0, 1\}$  and splits  $M^b$  into k shares  $M_1^b, \ldots, M_k^b$ .
  - (b) For  $j \in [k] \setminus \{i\}$ , it computes a normal ElGamal encryption of  $M_i^b$ ,  $\psi_i^b$ .
  - (c) For j = i, it sets  $\psi_i^b := (g_{\mathsf{tag}_i^*}^y, g_{\mathsf{tag}_i^*}^{M_i^b} g_{\mathsf{tag}_i^*}^z)$ .
  - (d) It sets  $\mathbf{c}^b := (\psi_1^b, \dots \psi_k^b)$ .
- 6. If  $\mathcal{A}$ 's guess matches b, then it outputs 1. Otherwise, it outputs 0.

First, note that by the information theoretic security of the secret sharing scheme, even though  $\mathcal{A}$ , by querying  $\mathcal{H}_S$ , can obtain the exponent  $t_j$  and thus totally recover some of (or even all) the shares  $M_j^b$  for  $j \in [k] \setminus \{i\}$ , it does not gain any information about  $M_i^b$  by these queries. Thus, the guess of  $\mathcal{A}$  for  $M^b$  is independent given any collection of shares that does not include  $M_i^b$ .

{51}------------------------------------------------

The proof is completed similarly to the reduction of ElGamal IND-CPA security to the DHH assumption. Namely, let  $\frac{1}{2} + \delta$  be the advantage of  $\mathcal{A}$  in breaking the hiding property of  $\mathsf{CS^F}$ . Next, assume that  $\mathcal{B}$  gets a valid DDH tuple  $(g_{\mathsf{tag}_i^*}, g_{\mathsf{tag}_i^*}^x, g_{\mathsf{tag}_i^*}^y, g_{\mathsf{tag}_i^*}^{xy})$ , i.e. z = xy. In this case,  $\mathcal{B}$  sets  $\psi_i^b$  so that it perfectly emulates the hiding game. On the other hand, if z is totally random, then the value  $\psi_i^b := (g_{\mathsf{tag}_i^*}^y, g_{\mathsf{tag}_i^*}^{M_i^b} g_{\mathsf{tag}_i^*}^z)$  is randomly distributed, independently from b.

We conclude that

$$\left| \Pr\left[ x, y \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \ \mathbb{Z}_q : \mathcal{B}(g_{\mathsf{tag}_i^*}, g_{\mathsf{tag}_i^*}^x, g_{\mathsf{tag}_i^*}^y, g_{\mathsf{tag}_i^*}^{xy}) = 1 \right] - \right.$$

$$\left. \Pr\left[ x, y, z \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \ \mathbb{Z}_q : \mathcal{B}(g_{\mathsf{tag}_i^*}, g_{\mathsf{tag}_i^*}^x, g_{\mathsf{tag}_i^*}^y, g_{\mathsf{tag}_i^*}^z) = 1 \right] \right| = \left| \left( \frac{1}{2} + \delta \right) - \frac{1}{2} \right| = \delta .$$

Namely, the distinguishing advantage of  $\mathcal{B}$  is also  $\delta$ .

# <span id="page-51-0"></span>C.3 The Lapidot-Shamir (LS) fully input-delayed protocol

The LS protocol is a fully input-delayed protocol, as defined in Definition 5, i.e. both the prover and the simulator only need to know the size of the statement in order to produce the first move, while the actual statement is only needed for the third move. The LS protocol is a proof of knowledge of a Hamiltonian cycle HC of a given graph G = (V, E) with size |G|. Consistently with the syntax of a  $\Sigma$ -protocol in Section 2, an LS proof consists of (1) the LS prover pair of algorithms  $(a, \operatorname{st}_P) \leftarrow \operatorname{LS.Prv}_1(|G|)$  and  $z \leftarrow \operatorname{LS.Prv}_2(\operatorname{st}_P, r, G, HC)$ , (2) the LS verifier algorithm  $\operatorname{LS.V}(a, G)$  that outputs a single bit challenge  $r \in \{0, 1\}$ , and (3) the verification algorithm  $\operatorname{LS.V}(a, r, z)$  that outputs 0/1.

Description. More concretely, the protocol works as follows:

- LS.Prv<sub>1</sub>(|G|): In the first move, the prover picks a random cycle RC with |V| vertices and commits to RC in terms of a  $|V| \times |V|$  adjacency matrix in an element-wise function. Then, it utilizes a perfectly binding commitment scheme to output the commitment to the adjacency matrix of RC as the first message a and a state  $\mathsf{st}_P \in \{0,1\}^*$ .
  - LS.V(a,G): The verifier sends a single bit challenge  $r \in \{0,1\}$ .
- LS.Prv<sub>2</sub>(st<sub>P</sub>, r, G, HC): In the third move, the prover takes as input the state st<sub>P</sub>  $\in \{0,1\}^*$ , the challenge  $r \in \{0,1\}$ , the statement G = (V,E) and the corresponding witness (Hamiltonian cycle) HC. If r = 0, then it opens the committed RC outputs and sends them as the response z; otherwise, it finds a permutation  $\pi$  that maps the vertices of G to the adjacency matrix of RC such that RC is the Hamiltonian cycle HC, using a (perfectly binding) commitment scheme. It then opens all the committed adjacency matrix RC elements that correspond to non-edges of G. It outputs  $\pi$  and the opening to the adjacency matrix as the response z.

{52}------------------------------------------------

• LS.Verify(G, a, r, z): it takes as input the statement x and the transcript (a, r, z), and it outputs 0/1, if the selected openings of the commitments to RC are consistent.

Security. The LS protocol satisfies input-delayed simulation, because there exists a simulator  $\mathsf{LS.Sim} = (\mathsf{LS.Sim}_1, \, \mathsf{LS.Sim}_2) \, \mathsf{s.t.}$  for any challenge r,  $\mathsf{LS.Sim}$  can simulate a transcript that is computationally indistinguishable from the real one in an input-delayed manner.  $\mathsf{LS.Sim} = (\mathsf{LS.Sim}_1, \, \mathsf{LS.Sim}_2)$  is defined as follows:

- LS.Sim<sub>1</sub>(r, |G|): the simulator's output depends on the challenge r as follows:
  - If r = 0, then it acts as LS.Prv<sub>1</sub>. Namely, it picks a random cycle RC and outputs a commitment to the adjacency matrix of RC as a simulated first message a.
  - If r=1, then it picks a random cycle RC, but now a is a commitment of an all-zero  $|V|\times |V|$  matrix.

In both cases, t he simulator state  $\mathsf{st}_{\mathsf{Sim}}$  contains a, r and all the decommitment information for the committed matrix.

- LS. $Sim_2(st_{Sim}, G)$ :
  - If r = 0, then it acts as LS.Prv<sub>2</sub>. I.e., it outputs z as the opening to RC.
  - If r=1, then it outputs z as a pair of (i) the permutation that maps RC to the vertices of G (in a fixed order), and (ii) the openings to the committed all-zero matrix that correspond to non-edges of G.

Note for r=0, LS.Sim acts a real prover therefore it produces transcripts that are identically distributed to the real ones. For r=1, the first message a and the permutation included in z are identically distributed to the ones in a real transcript, since RC is randomly chosen. Hence, sHVZK relies on the hiding property of the commitments between the real and the simulated adjacency matrix for RC.

Given Lemma 1 and the description of  $LS.Sim = (LS.Sim_1, LS.Sim_2)$ , we prove the existence of a fully input-delayed  $\Sigma$ -protocol for any  $\mathbf{NP}$  language that is secure against helper-aided adversaries.

**Proposition 1.** Let  $\mathbf{F} = \{f_{\mathsf{tag}} : \mathbb{Z}_{q_{\mathsf{tag}}} \longrightarrow G_{\mathsf{tag}}\}_{\mathsf{tag} \in \{0,1\}^{\lambda}}$  such that the DDH problem is adaptively hard in  $\mathbf{F}$ , and let  $\mathbf{H} = \{\mathcal{H}_S\}_{S \subset \{0,1\}^{\lambda}}$  be the associated helper family defined in Fig. 1.

Let  $\{\mathsf{tag}_1^*, \ldots, \mathsf{tag}_k^*\} \subset \{0,1\}^{\lambda}$  be a tag collection. Then, for every  $\mathcal{L} \in \mathbf{NP}$ , there exists a fully input-delayed  $\Sigma$ -protocol for  $\mathcal{L}$  that achieves (i) (perfect) completeness, (ii) special soundness and (iii) sHVZK against PPT adversaries with access to any member  $\mathcal{H}_S$  of  $\mathbf{H}$  such that  $\{\mathsf{tag}_1^*, \ldots, \mathsf{tag}_k^*\} \not\subset S$ .

*Proof.* Let  $\mathsf{CS^F} = (\mathsf{CS}.\mathsf{Gen^F}, \mathsf{CS}.\mathsf{Com^F}, \mathsf{CS}.\mathsf{Ver^F})$  be the commitment scheme presented in  $\mathsf{C}.2$ . By the  $\mathsf{NP}$ -completeness of the Hamiltonian Cycle problem, it suffices to show that the LS protocol over  $\mathsf{CS^F}$  satisfies the properties in Proposition 1. The proof is as in the standard proof the LS protocol, except from sHVZK where reference to helper access is necessary.

**Perfect completeness**: follows directly from the correctness of  $\mathsf{CS}.\mathsf{Gen}^\mathbf{F}$  that in turn, is based on the correctness of  $\mathsf{ElGamal}$  encryption.

{53}------------------------------------------------

**Special soundness**: follows from the perfect binding property of  $\mathsf{CS}^\mathbf{F}$ . Namely, given two accepting transcripts  $(G,a,0,z_0)$  and  $(G,a,1,z_1)$  allows the extraction of the witness HC by LS.Ext. This is true since for r=0 one gets the random cycle RC and for r=1 one gets the permutation that maps the random cycle in the actual cycle HC. Note that a single run of the LS proof has soundness error 1/2 and needs to be repeated  $\lambda$  times for soundness amplification to  $2^{-\lambda}$ .

**sHVZK**: let S such that  $\{\mathsf{tag}_1^*, \ldots, \mathsf{tag}_k^*\} \not\subset S$  and  $\mathcal{A}$  be a PPT adversary with access to  $\mathcal{H}_S$  that breaks the sHVZK property of LS. Since for challenge r=0, the simulator LS.Sim acts exactly as the actual prover, we have that for challenge r=1,  $\mathcal{A}$  can distinguish a simulated transcript from a real one on challenge 1 with some non-negligible advantage  $\alpha(\lambda)$ . W.l.o.g. (otherwise we can flip the output of  $\mathcal{A}$ ), assume that

<span id="page-53-0"></span>
$$\Pr\left[(a,\mathsf{st}_P) \leftarrow \mathsf{LS.Prv}_1(|G|); z \leftarrow \mathsf{LS.Prv}_2(\mathsf{st}_P,1,G,HC) : \mathcal{A}(G,a,1,z) = 1\right] \geq \\ \geq \Pr\left[(a,\mathsf{st}_{\mathsf{Sim}}) \leftarrow \mathsf{LS.Sim}_1(1,|G|); z \leftarrow \mathsf{LS.Sim}_2(\mathsf{st}_{\mathsf{Sim}},G) : \mathcal{A}(G,a,1,z) = 1\right] + \alpha(\lambda) \ . \tag{8}$$

We construct a PPT adversary  $\mathcal{B}$  with access to  $\mathcal{H}_S$  against the computational hiding property of  $\mathsf{CS^F}$ . Namely, on input a commitment key  $\mathsf{ck}$ ,  $\mathcal{B}$  operates as follows:

- 1. It specifies a graph G with vertex set V and Hamiltonian cycle HC w.r.t. the security parameter  $\lambda$  implied by  $\mathsf{ck}$ .
- 2. It provides two multi-challenge vectors  $M_0 = (0, ..., 0)$  and  $M_1 = (1, ..., 1)$  consisting of |V| elements. By a standard hybrid argument, we can refer to multi-challege hiding, as it is equivalent to single-challenge hiding up to a negligible error.
- 3. It replies to every query of  $\mathcal{A}$  for  $\mathcal{H}_S$ , simply by forwarding the query and returning  $\mathcal{H}_S$ 's response.
- 4. It receives a vector of |V| commitments to challenge bit b. Then, it runs an execution of LS as the prover does for challenge r=1 with the following modification:  $\mathcal{B}$  commits to the adjacency matrix of RC by plugging in the |V| commitments to b in the matrix entries that correspond to the edges of RC.
- 5. It returns the response of A.

Observe that when b=1, then  $\mathcal{B}$  interacts as a real prover, whereas when b=0 it outputs a transcript identically distributed to the simulated ones. In

{54}------------------------------------------------

both cases, this is on condition the challenge is 1. Thus, by Eq. (8), it holds that

$$\begin{split} &\Pr\left[\mathcal{B} \text{ wins}\right] = \\ &= \frac{1}{2} \cdot \Pr\left[\mathcal{B} \text{ wins } \middle| \ b = 1\right] + \frac{1}{2} \cdot \Pr\left[\mathcal{B} \text{ wins } \middle| \ b = 0\right] = \\ &= \frac{1}{2} \cdot \Pr\left[\mathcal{B} \text{ outputs } 1 \middle| \ b = 1\right] + \frac{1}{2} \cdot \Pr\left[\mathcal{B} \text{ outputs } 0 \middle| \ b = 0\right] = \\ &= \frac{1}{2} + \frac{1}{2} \cdot \left(\Pr\left[\mathcal{B} \text{ outputs } 1 \middle| \ b = 1\right] - \Pr\left[\mathcal{B} \text{ outputs } 1 \middle| \ b = 0\right]\right) = \\ &= \frac{1}{2} + \frac{1}{2} \cdot \left(\Pr\left[\mathcal{A} \text{ outputs } 1 \middle| \ b = 1\right] - \Pr\left[\mathcal{A} \text{ outputs } 1 \middle| \ b = 0\right]\right) \geq \\ &\geq \frac{1}{2} + \frac{\alpha(\lambda)}{2} \;. \end{split}$$

Therefore,  $\mathcal{B}$  wins with non-negligible distinguishing advantage which contradicts to the hiding property of  $\mathsf{CS}^\mathbf{F}$  proven in Lemma 1. This completes the proof.  $\square$ 

#### <span id="page-54-0"></span>C.4 Proof of Theorem 2

*Proof.* Crowd-verifiable Completeness. In the following argumentation, we assume that the von Neumann sampling process for the computation of the coalescence outputs will not fail. By Theorem 1, this is something that happens with  $1 - \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$  probability, so we may include the  $\mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$  failure error in the total error bound at the end of the completeness proof.

For a statement  $x \in \mathcal{L} \cap \{0,1\}^{\text{poly}(\lambda)}$  and a witness w for x, the completeness of  $\Sigma.\Pi$  gives us that

$$\begin{split} \Pr[(a,\mathsf{st}) \leftarrow \varSigma. \mathsf{Prv}_1(x,w); e \leftarrow \varSigma. V(x,a); r \leftarrow \varSigma. \mathsf{Prv}_2(x,w,e,\mathsf{st}) : \\ \varSigma. \mathsf{Verify}(x,a,e,z) = 0] \leq \delta(\lambda). \end{split}$$

Let  $\mathcal{A}$  be a (not necessarily PPT) adversary against the completeness of the CVZK protocol CVZK. $\Pi$  that may tamper at most  $t_1$  verifier challenges, i.e. it can flip at most  $t_1$  bits of the random challenge  $C = (c_1, \ldots, c_n)$ . By engaging in the completeness experiment  $\mathbf{Expt}^{\mathsf{CVCompl}}_{(t_1, \mathcal{A}, \mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{corr}})}(1^{\lambda}, x, w)$  (as defined in Def. 3), the adversary will win only if it manages to output an adversarial challenge  $\hat{C}$  s.t. a non-accepting CVZK transcript  $(A, \hat{C}, Z)$  is generated.

In the third round of  $\mathsf{CVZK}.\Pi$ , the prover algorithm  $\mathsf{CVZK}.\mathsf{Prv}_2(x,w,A,\hat{C},\mathsf{st}_P)$  will generate the n-bit string  $E = \hat{C} \oplus R$ , where R is a truly random n-bit string produced by  $\mathsf{CVZK}.\mathsf{Prv}_1(x,w)$  and included in its state  $\mathsf{st}_P$ . So,  $E := (e_1,\ldots,e_{\log\lambda})$  is uniformly distributed. Note that this holds  $\mathit{unconditionally}$ , i.e., regardless of  $\mathcal{A}$ 's strategy.

Therefore, the strings  $e_1, \ldots, e_{\log \lambda}$  that will be used as challenges for the  $\log \lambda$  independent executions of  $\mathsf{CVZK}.\Pi$  are uniformly random. By the completeness

{55}------------------------------------------------

and uniformity of the challenge of  $\Sigma.\Pi$  and the union bound, we have that

<span id="page-55-2"></span>
$$\Pr\left[(a_{i},\mathsf{st}_{i}) \leftarrow \Sigma.\mathsf{Prv}_{1}(x,w), i \in [\log \lambda]; (e_{1},\ldots,e_{\log \lambda}) \leftarrow \mathbb{U}_{n}; \\ z_{i} \leftarrow \Sigma.\mathsf{Prv}_{2}(x,w,e_{i},\mathsf{st}_{i}), i \in [\log \lambda] : \bigwedge_{i \in [\log \lambda]} \Sigma.\mathsf{Verify}(x,a_{i},e_{i},z_{i}) = 1\right] \\ \geq 1 - \sum_{i \in [\log \lambda]} \Pr\left[(a_{i},\mathsf{st}_{i}) \leftarrow \Sigma.\mathsf{Prv}_{1}(x,w); e_{i} \leftarrow \mathbb{U}_{n/\log \lambda}; \\ z_{i} \leftarrow \Sigma.\mathsf{Prv}_{2}(x,w,e_{i},\mathsf{st}_{i}) : \Sigma.\mathsf{Verify}(x,a_{i},e_{i},z_{i}) = 0\right] \geq \\ \geq 1 - \delta(\lambda) \log \lambda .$$

$$(9)$$

To argue about the completeness of the input-delayed proofs, we first make use of the public samplability property of  $\mathbf{F}$  (cf. property (1) of Definition 2) with error  $\epsilon(\cdot)$ . Namely, for every  $\mathsf{tag}_{\ell}$ ,  $\ell \in [n]$ , it holds that

<span id="page-55-0"></span>
$$\Pr\left[\omega \leftarrow \mathbb{U}_{\lambda/\log^2 \lambda} : \mathsf{IM}(\mathsf{tag}_{\ell}, \omega) \in Y_{\mathsf{tag}}\right] \ge 1 - \epsilon(\lambda) \ . \tag{10}$$

By Eq. (10), at least  $(1 - \epsilon_{\ell}(\lambda))2^{\frac{\lambda}{\log^2 \lambda}}$  strings in  $\{0,1\}^{\frac{\lambda}{\log^2 \lambda}}$  lead to the sampling of a valid statement for  $\mathcal{L}^*_{\mathsf{tag}_{\ell}}$ . We argue that by the construction of the coalescence  $F(\cdot)$  over the strongly resilient function  $f_{\mathsf{res}}$ , for every  $\ell \in [n], j \in [\log \lambda]$ , the output of  $\mathsf{IM}(\mathsf{tag}_{\ell}, d_j)$  is a valid statement  $x^*_{\ell,j} \in \mathcal{L}^*_{\mathsf{tag}_{\ell}}$ .

Let  $(G_1, \ldots, G_{\log \lambda})$  be the grouping of the bits of the input to the coalescence function that produce the output of  $\lambda/\log^2 \lambda$ - bits strings  $d_1, \ldots, d_{\log \lambda}$ . Note that the adversary breaks completeness even if it manages to tamper the coalescence output of a single group so that it leads to the sampling of an invalid value (non-statement). Thus, it suffices to show that  $\mathcal{A}$  has  $\operatorname{negl}(\lambda)$  success probability, even if it concentrates all its tampering power of  $t_1$  bits, upon a single group, say  $G_j$ . As in Theorem 1 proof, let  $B_{j,1}, \ldots, B_{j,\lambda}$  denote the blocks inside the group  $G_j$ .  $b_{j,1}, \ldots, b_{j,\lambda}$  be the bit sequence that, when given as input to the von Neumann rejection sampling process, produces  $d_j$ . Namely,  $b_{j,k}$  is the output bit of  $f_{\text{res}}(B_{j,k})$ .

Next, assume that  $\mathcal{A}$  selects  $t_{i,j}$  bits to corrupt for the block  $B_{i,j}$ , and  $\forall i$ :  $\sum_{j=1}^{\lambda} t_{i,j} = t_1$ . Let  $\mathsf{Inf}_{i,j}$  be the event that over the input coins of  $B_{i,j}$  the adversary  $\mathcal{A}$  influences the output bit  $b_{i,j} \leftarrow f_{\mathsf{res}}(B_{i,j})$ , where  $i \in [\log \lambda], j \in [\lambda]$ . By the  $(\Theta(\frac{\log^2 m}{m}))$ -strong resilience of  $f_{\mathsf{res}}$ , if we set  $m = \frac{n}{\lambda \log \lambda}$  (the input block length), then each corrupted bit in  $B_{i,j}$  has probability  $\Theta(\frac{\log^3 n}{n^{1-\frac{1}{\gamma}}})$  to influence the output bit of  $B_{i,j}$ . By the union bound, the latter implies that

<span id="page-55-1"></span>
$$\Pr\left[\mathsf{Inf}_{i,j}\right] = \Theta\left(t_{i,j} \cdot \frac{\log^3 n}{n^{1-\frac{1}{\gamma}}}\right). \tag{11}$$

Now, let X be the random variable that counts the number of blocks from  $B_{i,1}, \ldots, B_{i,\lambda}$  which output bit has been influenced. It is easy to see that X is

{56}------------------------------------------------

the sum of  $\lambda$  Bernoulli trials with success probability  $\Pr\left[\mathsf{Inf}_{i,1}\right], \ldots, \Pr\left[\mathsf{Inf}_{i,\lambda}\right],$  respectively. Thus, by Eq. (11), the mean of X is

$$\mu = \mathbb{E}[X] = \sum_{j=1}^{\lambda} \Theta\left(t_{i,j} \cdot \frac{\log^3 n}{n^{1-\frac{1}{\gamma}}}\right) = \Theta\left(t_1 \cdot \frac{\log^3 n}{n^{1-\frac{1}{\gamma}}}\right).$$

By requiring that  $t_1 \leq \frac{n^{1-\frac{1}{\gamma}}}{\log^2 n}$ , we get that  $\mu = \Theta(\log n)$ . Thus, by the Chernoff bounds, we have that asymptotically

$$\Pr\left[X \ge \log^2 n\right] \le \Pr\left[X \ge \mu \log^{1/2} n\right] \le e^{-\Theta(\log^{3/2} n)} = \mathsf{negl}(\lambda) \ . \tag{12}$$

So with  $1 - \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$ ,  $\mathcal{A}$  can not influence more than  $\Theta(\log^2 n)$  out of the  $\lambda$  bits of the sequence  $b_{j,1}, \ldots, b_{j,\lambda}$ . Since to produce a bit of  $d_j$ , we need at least two input bits in the von Neumann sampling process, we deduce that  $\mathcal{A}$  can neither influence more than  $\Theta(\log^2 n)$  out of the  $\lambda/\log^2 \lambda$  bits of  $d_j$ .

Recall that the  $\lambda/\log^2 \lambda - \Theta(\log^2 n)$  uninfluenced bits of  $d_j$  are uniformly random. Therefore, given the adversarial tampering the output is randomly chose among a subset of  $2^{\frac{\lambda}{\log^2 \lambda} - \Theta(\log^2 n)}$  strings.

In the worst case (hence, optimal in terms of adversarial strategy), these  $2^{\frac{\lambda}{\log^2 \lambda} - \Theta(\log^2 n)}$  strings contain all the "bad" ones that lead to an invalid value (non-statement). By Eq. (10), the bad strings can be no more than  $\epsilon(\lambda) 2^{\frac{\lambda}{\log^2 \lambda}}$  in total. Thus, the probability of randomly hitting a bad string is upper bounded by

$$\frac{\epsilon(\lambda)2^{\frac{\lambda}{\log^2 \lambda}}}{2^{\frac{\lambda}{\log^2 \lambda} - \Theta(\log^2 n)}} = \epsilon(\lambda)2^{\Theta(\log^2 n)}.$$

Recall that the above bound holds for every  $\ell \in [n] \forall j \in [\log \lambda]$ . Thus, by the union bound, we have that

$$\Pr\left[\bigwedge_{\ell \in [n]} \bigwedge_{j \in [\log \lambda]} x_{\ell,j}^* = \mathsf{IM}(\mathsf{tag}_{\ell}, d_j) \in \mathcal{L}_{\mathsf{tag}_{\ell}}^*\right]$$

$$\geq 1 - n \log \lambda \epsilon(\lambda) 2^{\Theta(\log^2 n)} .$$
(13)

<span id="page-56-0"></span>Given the validity of input-delayed statements, the completeness of all proofs for the validity of  $x_{\ell,j}^*$ ,  $\ell \in [n], j \in [\log \lambda]$  follows directly from the perfect completeness of the  $\mathsf{InD}.\Pi$  proofs.

By Eq. (9) and (13) and including the  $\operatorname{negl}(\lambda)$  failure error in von Neumann sampling, we conclude that for every  $t_1 \leq \frac{n^{1-\frac{1}{\gamma}}}{\log^2 n}$ ,  $\operatorname{CVZK}.\Pi$  has  $(t_1, \epsilon_1)$ -completeness, where  $\epsilon_1(\lambda) := \delta(\lambda) \log \lambda + n \log \lambda \epsilon(\lambda) 2^{\Theta(\log^2 n)} + \operatorname{negl}(\lambda)$ .

Crowd-verifiable soundness. Given  $t_2$ -crowd verifiable validity that will be shown below, crowd verifiable soundness is easily deduced. Specifically, assume

{57}------------------------------------------------

that there is a non-negligible function  $\alpha(\cdot)$  and an (unbounded) adversary  $\mathcal{A}$  s.t. for some  $x \in \{0,1\}^{\mathsf{poly}(\lambda)}$ 

$$\Pr\left[\mathbf{Expt}^{\mathsf{CVSound}}_{(t_2,\mathcal{A},\mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{corr}})}(1^{\lambda},x) = 1\right] \geq \alpha(\lambda) \ .$$

Then, by  $t_2$ -crowd verifiable validity, then we have that  $x \in \mathcal{L}$  with non-negligible probability  $\beta(\lambda)$ , as the CVZK extractor utilizing  $\mathcal{A}$  can output a witness for  $x \in \mathcal{L}$  with non-negligible probability. Therefore for every  $x \in \{0,1\}^{\mathsf{poly}(\lambda)} \setminus \mathcal{L}$  and every adversary  $\mathcal{A}$  there is a negligible function  $\epsilon_{x,\mathcal{A}}(\cdot)$  s.t.

$$\Pr\left[\mathbf{Expt}^{\mathsf{CVSound}}_{(t_2,\mathcal{A},\mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{corr}})}(1^{\lambda},x)=1\right] \leq \epsilon_{x,\mathcal{A}}(\lambda)$$
.

Since for every  $\lambda$ , the set  $\{\epsilon_{x,\mathcal{A}}(\lambda)\}_{x,\mathcal{A}}$  is lower bounded (e.g., by 0), by the supremum axiom of the real numbers we can define the function  $\epsilon(\cdot)$ 

$$\epsilon_2(\lambda) := \inf(\{\epsilon_{x,\mathcal{A}}(\lambda)\}_{x,\mathcal{A}}).$$

Clearly,  $\epsilon(\cdot)$  is negligible. Besides, by the definition of  $\epsilon_{x,\mathcal{A}}(\cdot)$ , we deduce that  $\mathsf{CVZK}.\Pi$  achieves  $(t_2,\epsilon_2)$ -crowd verifiable soundness.

**Crowd-verifiable validity.** Let  $\mathcal{A}$  be an adversary against the crowd verifiable validity of  $\mathsf{CVZK}.\mathcal{I}$  that acts as a malicious prover, corrupts up to  $t_2$  verifiers and engages in  $\mathsf{Expt}^{\mathsf{CVSound}}_{(t_2,\mathcal{A},\mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{corr}})}(1^{\lambda})$ . For some statement x, and subset  $\mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{corr}} \subset [n]$  we define

$$p_x := \Pr\left[\mathbf{Expt}^{\mathsf{CVSound}}_{(t_2,\mathcal{A},\mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{corr}})}(1^{\lambda},x) = 1\right].$$

We construct a (candidate) PPT extractor algorithm CVZK.Ext for CVZK. $\Pi$  that emulates  $\mathbf{Expt}^{\mathsf{CVSound}}_{(t_2,\mathcal{A},\mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{corr}})}(1^\lambda,x)$  with standard rewinding and utilizes the extractor of  $\Sigma.\Pi$ , denoted by  $\Sigma.\mathsf{Ext}$ , to extract a witness for x, given the publicly samplable adaptive one-way function family  $\mathbf{F}$ . Then, we will analyze the success probability for CVZK.Ext.

The algorithm CVZK.Ext given the code of  $\mathcal{A}$ , on input x and the set of indices of corrupted users  $\mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{corr}}$ , executes the following steps:

- 1. It starts  $\mathbf{Expt}^{\mathsf{CVSound}}_{(t_2,\mathcal{A},\mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{corr}})}(1^{\lambda},x)$ , playing the role of honest verifiers.
- 2. It obtains  $(A := (\{a_i\}_{i \in [\log \lambda]}, \{a_\ell^*\}_{\ell \in [n]}))$  from  $\mathcal{A}(x, \mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{corr}})$ .
- 3. For  $\ell \in [n]$ , it chooses a random bit  $c_{\ell}$  to generate challenge  $C := (c_1, \ldots, c_n) \in \{0, 1\}^n$ .
- 4. It obtains tampered challenge  $\hat{C} := \langle \hat{c}_1, \dots, \hat{c}_n \rangle$  and response  $Z := (E, \{z_i\}_{i \in [\log \lambda]}, \{z_{\ell,j}^*\}_{\ell \in [n]}^{j \in [\log \lambda]})$  from  $\mathcal{A}(x, \langle c_\ell \rangle_{\ell \in \mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{corr}}})$ .
- 5. If  $\mathsf{CVZK}.\mathsf{Verify}(x,A,\hat{C},Z)=1$ , then it rewinds  $\mathcal{A}$  at the beginning of the challenge step providing  $\mathcal{A}$  with a new challenge  $\langle c'_{\ell} \rangle_{\ell \in \mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{corr}}}$  derived from a fresh challenge  $C' := (c'_1, \ldots, c'_n) \in \{0,1\}^n$ , where C' is computed as follows:
- (a). Let  $(G_1, \ldots, G_{\lambda \log \lambda})$  be the partition of C and  $(G'_1, \ldots, G'_{\lambda \log \lambda})$  be the partition of C' into  $\log \lambda$  groups of  $\frac{n}{\log \lambda}$  bits.
- (b). CVZK.Ext chooses randomly an index  $i^* \in [\log \lambda]$ .
- (c). For every  $i \in [\log \lambda] \setminus i^*$ , it chooses  $G_i$  uniformly at random.

{58}------------------------------------------------

(d). It fixes  $G'_{i*} = G_{i*}$ .

If CVZK. Verify $(x, A, \hat{C}, Z) = 0$ , then CVZK. Ext aborts.

- 6. It obtains new tampered challenge  $\hat{C}' := \langle \hat{c}'_1, \dots, \hat{c}'_n \rangle$  and new response Z' := $(E', \{z_i'\}_{i \in [\log \lambda]}, \{z_{\ell,j}'^*\}_{\ell \in [n]}^{j \in [\log \lambda]}) \text{ from } \mathcal{A}(x, \langle c_1', \dots, c_n' \rangle).$
- 7. If CVZK. Verify $(x, A, \hat{C}', Z') = 1$ , then
- (a). Let  $F(\hat{C}) = (\hat{d}_1, \dots, \hat{d}_{\log \lambda})$ , and  $F(\hat{C}') = (\hat{d}'_1, \dots, \hat{d}'_{\log \lambda})$ , be the coalescence images. Parse E, E' as  $(e_1, \ldots, e_{\log \lambda})$  and  $(e'_1, \ldots, e'_{\log \lambda})$ , respectively.
- (b). If there is an  $i_0 \in [\log \lambda]$  s.t.  $e_{i_0} \neq e'_{i_0}$ , then it runs  $\Sigma$ .Ext on input the two valid transcripts  $(x, a_{i_0}, e_{i_0}, z_{i_0})$  and  $(x, a'_{i_0}, e'_{i_0}, z'_{i_0})$  and receives the output w of  $\Sigma$ .Ext. Then, CVZK.Ext returns w as a valid witness for x.
- (c). Otherwise, it holds that  $(e_1, \ldots, e_{\log \lambda}) = (e'_1, \ldots, e'_{\log \lambda})$ . Then, CVZK.Ext aborts.

If CVZK. Verify $(x, A, \hat{C}', Z') = 0$ , then CVZK. Ext aborts.

By the above description and the special soundness of  $\Sigma.\Pi$ , CVZK.Ext will certainly output a valid witness for x if the following events happen:

- (i).  $\mathsf{CVZK}.\mathsf{Verify}(x,A,\hat{C},Z) = \mathsf{CVZK}.\mathsf{Verify}(x,A,\hat{C}',Z') = 1$ , and
- (ii).  $E \neq E'$ .

We lower bound the probability that these events happen via two claims. We begin by making use of the coalescence function  $F(\cdot)$ . Specifically, recall that Definition 4 refers to unbounded adversaries, thus it captures the state of  $\mathcal{A}$  upon receiving a randomly chosen CVZK challenge C. Therefore,  $(d_1, \ldots, d_{\log \lambda}) \leftarrow$  $F(\hat{C})$  is a valid output for a run of the experiment  $\mathbf{Expt}^{\mathsf{Coal}}_{(t,\mathcal{A},\mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{corr}})}(1^{\lambda})$  in Definition 4. The latter implies that there exist events  $G_1, \ldots G_m$  from the probability space of  $\mathbf{Expt}^{\mathsf{CVSound}}_{(t_2,\mathcal{A},\mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{corr}})}(1^{\lambda},x)$  such that for the following two conditions hold: (1)  $\Pr[\wedge_{i=1}^{\log \lambda} \neg \mathsf{G}_i] = \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$ , and

- (2) for all  $i \in [\log \lambda]$  and  $\mathcal{I}_{corr}$  output by  $\mathcal{A}$ , the random variable  $(d_i|\mathsf{G}_i)$  is statistically  $\operatorname{\mathsf{negl}}(\lambda)$ -close to  $\mathbb{U}_{\lambda/\log^2 \lambda}$

By the construction of  $F(\cdot)$  (cf. also proof of Theorem 1), each  $G_i$  denotes the event that  $\mathcal{A}$  does not influence any of the  $\lambda$  bits output by the blocks of group  $G_i$ , where  $(G_1, \ldots, G_{\log \lambda}) = C$ . Thus, by condition (1) we have that

<span id="page-58-0"></span>
$$\Pr\left[\left(\mathbf{Expt}_{(t_2,\mathcal{A})}^{\mathsf{CVSound}}(1^{\lambda},x)=1\right) \wedge \left(\vee_{i=1}^{\log \lambda} \mathsf{G}_i\right)\right] = p_x - \mathsf{negl}(\lambda) \ . \tag{14}$$

By Eq. (14) and the union bound, we get that

<span id="page-58-1"></span>
$$p_{x} - \mathsf{negl}(\lambda) = \Pr\left[\left(\mathbf{Expt}_{(t_{2},\mathcal{A})}^{\mathsf{CVSound}}(1^{\lambda}, x) = 1\right) \land \left(\vee_{i=1}^{\log \lambda} \mathsf{G}_{i}\right)\right] =$$

$$= \Pr\left[\vee_{i=1}^{\log \lambda} \left(\left(\mathbf{Expt}_{(t_{2},\mathcal{A})}^{\mathsf{CVSound}}(1^{\lambda}, x) = 1\right) \land \mathsf{G}_{i}\right)\right] \le$$

$$\le \sum_{i=1}^{\log \lambda} \left[\left(\mathbf{Expt}_{(t_{2},\mathcal{A})}^{\mathsf{CVSound}}(1^{\lambda}, x) = 1\right) \land \mathsf{G}_{i}\right)\right].$$
(15)

{59}------------------------------------------------

By Eq. (15) and an averaging argument, we conclude that there exists an  $i_0 \in [\log \lambda]$  and a negligible function  $\delta(\cdot)$  s.t.

<span id="page-59-1"></span><span id="page-59-0"></span>
$$\Pr\left[\left(\mathbf{Expt}_{(t_2,\mathcal{A})}^{\mathsf{CVSound}}(1^{\lambda}, x) = 1\right) \wedge \mathsf{G}_{i_0}\right] \ge \frac{p_x}{\log \lambda} - \delta(\lambda) \ . \tag{16}$$

Coming back to the setting dictated by the description of CVZK.Ext, we define as Uninf the event that in both runs (the first with challenge C and the rewinded run with challenge C' on condition that  $G_{i^*}$  is fixed) that CVZK.Ext performs the event  $G_{i^*}$ . We lower bound the probability Valid  $\wedge$  Uninf happens in the following claim.

Claim 5 
$$\Pr[\mathsf{Valid} \wedge \mathsf{Uninf}] \ge \frac{(p_x)^3}{8(\log \lambda)^4} - \mathsf{negl}(\lambda).$$

*Proof of claim:* We prove the claim via a combinatorial argument along the lines of the splitting Lemma. Let  $\mathbf{A}$  be the space of all first move messages A and  $\mathbf{R}$  be the space of all response messages  $(\hat{C}, Z)$  that A outputs during the execution of  $\mathbf{Expt}^{\mathsf{CVSound}}_{(t_2, A)}(1^{\lambda}, x)$ . For notation simplicity, the elements of  $\mathbf{A}$ ,  $\mathbf{R}$  are in one-to-one correspondence with the coins of A, where w.l.o.g. we assume that A always outputs well-formed messages.

For the index  $i_0$  guaranteed from Eq. (16), let Good.Tr<sub>x</sub> be the set of triples  $(A, C := (G_1, \ldots, G_{\log \lambda}), (\hat{C} := (\hat{G}_1, \ldots, \hat{G}_{\log \lambda}), Z)) \in \mathbf{A} \times (\{0, 1\}^{\frac{n}{\log \lambda}})^{\log \lambda} \times \mathbf{R}$  such that

- 1.  $\mathsf{CVZK}.\mathsf{Verify}(x,A,\hat{C},Z) = 1$  (i.e. the transcript  $(x,A,\hat{C},Z)$  is accepting), and
- 2. the output  $d_{i_0}$  of  $G_{i_0}$  is not influenced (i.e. running  $f_{res}$  on the blocks  $G_{i_0}$  and  $\hat{G}_{i_0}$  produces the same output  $d_{i_0}$ ).

Let Good.Run<sub>x</sub> be the set of pairs  $(A, G) \in \mathbf{A} \times \{0, 1\}^{\frac{n}{\log \lambda}}$  s.t.

$$\begin{split} \left| \left\{ (C, \hat{C}, Z) \; \middle| \; \left( \left( A, C := (G_1, \dots, G_{\log \lambda}), (\hat{C}, Z) \right) \in \mathsf{Good}.\mathsf{Tr}_x \right) \wedge \right. \\ \left. \wedge \left( G_{i_0} = G \right) \right\} \right| &\geq \frac{1}{2} \cdot \left( \frac{p_x}{\log \lambda} - \delta(\lambda) \right) \cdot \left( 2^{\frac{n}{\log \lambda}} \right)^{\log \lambda - 1} \cdot |\mathbf{R}| \; . \end{split}$$

Observe that CVZK.Ext samples the honest verifiers slightly differently than in  $\mathbf{Expt}^{\mathsf{CVSound}}_{(t_2,\mathcal{A},\mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{corr}})}(1^{\lambda},x)$ . Namely, it chooses n bits for all positions (including the ones in  $\mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{corr}}$ ) and then provides  $\mathcal{A}$  with only the honest verifiers' bits. It is straightforward that this way of sampling is (i) consistent with the coalescence experiment  $\mathbf{Expt}^{\mathsf{Coal}}_{(t,\mathcal{A},\mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{corr}})}(1^{\lambda})$  (cf. Definition 4), and (ii) that this leads to exactly the same distribution as choosing only the honest bits as in CVZK security experiments. Therefore, by Eq. (16) and the definition of  $\mathsf{Good}.\mathsf{Tr}_x$ ,  $\mathsf{Good}.\mathsf{Run}_x$ ,

{60}------------------------------------------------

we have that

<span id="page-60-0"></span>
$$\begin{split} &\left(\frac{p_x}{\log\lambda} - \delta(\lambda)\right) \cdot |\mathbf{A}| \cdot \left(2^{\frac{n}{\log\lambda}}\right)^{\log\lambda} \cdot |\mathbf{R}| \leq |\mathsf{Good}.\mathsf{Tr}_x| = \\ &= \sum_{(A,G)} \left| \left\{ (C,\hat{C},Z) \mid \left( (A,C,(\hat{C},Z)) \in \mathsf{Good}.\mathsf{Tr}_x \right) \wedge \left( G_{i_0} = G \right) \right\} \right| = \\ &= \sum_{(A,G) \in \mathsf{Good}.\mathsf{Run}_x} \left| \left\{ (C,\hat{C},Z) \mid \left( (A,C,(\hat{C},Z)) \in \mathsf{Good}.\mathsf{Tr}_x \right) \wedge \left( G_{i_0} = G \right) \right\} \right| + \\ &+ \sum_{(A,G) \notin \mathsf{Good}.\mathsf{Run}_x} \left| \left\{ (C,\hat{C},Z) \mid \left( (A,C,(\hat{C},Z)) \in \mathsf{Good}.\mathsf{Tr}_x \right) \wedge \left( G_{i_0} = G \right) \right\} \right| \Rightarrow \\ &\Rightarrow \left( \frac{p_x}{\log\lambda} - \delta(\lambda) \right) \cdot |\mathbf{A}| \cdot \left( 2^{\frac{n}{\log\lambda}} \right)^{\log\lambda} \cdot |\mathbf{R}| \\ &\leq |\mathsf{Good}.\mathsf{Run}_x| \cdot \left( 2^{\frac{n}{\log\lambda}} \right)^{\log\lambda-1} \cdot |\mathbf{R}| + \\ &+ \left( |\mathbf{A}| \cdot 2^{\frac{n}{\log\lambda}} - |\mathsf{Good}.\mathsf{Run}_x| \right) \frac{1}{2} \cdot \left( \frac{p_x}{\log\lambda} - \delta(\lambda) \right) \cdot \left( 2^{\frac{n}{\log\lambda}} \right)^{\log\lambda-1} \cdot |\mathbf{R}| \Rightarrow \\ &\Rightarrow \left( \frac{p_x}{\log\lambda} - \delta(\lambda) \right) \cdot |\mathbf{A}| \cdot \left( 2^{\frac{n}{\log\lambda}} \right)^{\log\lambda} \cdot |\mathbf{R}| \\ &\leq |\mathsf{Good}.\mathsf{Run}_x| \cdot \left( 2^{\frac{n}{\log\lambda}} \right)^{\log\lambda} \cdot |\mathbf{R}| + \\ &+ \left( |\mathbf{A}| \cdot 2^{\frac{n}{\log\lambda}} \right) \frac{1}{2} \cdot \left( \frac{p_x}{\log\lambda} - \delta(\lambda) \right) \cdot \left( 2^{\frac{n}{\log\lambda}} \right)^{\log\lambda-1} \cdot |\mathbf{R}| \Rightarrow \\ \Rightarrow |\mathsf{Good}.\mathsf{Run}_x| \geq \frac{1}{2} \cdot \left( \frac{p_x}{\log\lambda} - \delta(\lambda) \right) \cdot |\mathbf{A}| \cdot 2^{\frac{n}{\log\lambda}} \cdot \log\lambda \,. \end{split}$$

If in the first run, assume the pair  $(A, C := (G_1, \ldots, G_{\log \lambda}))$  is s.t. for some  $i_0, (A, G_{i_0}) \in \mathsf{Good}.\mathsf{Run}_x$ . Let  $\mathsf{Guess}$  be the event that by randomly choosing  $i^*$  in  $\mathsf{Step}\ 5(\mathsf{d})$ ,  $\mathsf{CVZK}.\mathsf{Ext}$  successfully guesses  $i^* = i_0$ . Then, by definition of  $\mathsf{Good}.\mathsf{Run}_x$ , and the description of  $\mathsf{CVZK}.\mathsf{Ext}$  (Steps  $5(\mathsf{a})$ -(d)), we have that

<span id="page-60-1"></span>
$$\Pr\left[\mathsf{Valid} \land \mathsf{Uninf} \mid (A, G_{i_0}) \in \mathsf{Good}.\mathsf{Run}_x, \mathsf{Guess}\right] \ge \frac{1}{4} \cdot \left(\frac{p_x}{\log \lambda} - \delta(\lambda)\right)^2. \tag{18}$$

By Eq. (17) and (18), the negligibility of  $\delta(\cdot)$ , and the fact that  $\Pr[\mathsf{Guess}] = \frac{1}{\log \lambda}$ , where the guess is independently at random, we conclude that

$$\begin{split} &\Pr[\mathsf{Valid} \land \mathsf{Uninf}] \\ &\geq \Pr\left[\mathsf{Valid} \land \mathsf{Uninf} \land \left( (A,G_{i_0}) \in \mathsf{Good}.\mathsf{Run}_x \right) \land \mathsf{Guess} \right] = \\ &= \Pr\left[\mathsf{Valid} \land \mathsf{Uninf} \mid (A,G_{i_0}) \in \mathsf{Good}.\mathsf{Run}_x, \mathsf{Guess} \right] \cdot \\ &\quad \cdot \Pr\left[ (A,G_{i_0}) \in \mathsf{Good}.\mathsf{Run}_x \right] \cdot \Pr\left[ \mathsf{Guess} \right] \geq \\ &\geq \frac{1}{4} \cdot \left( \frac{p_x}{\log \lambda} - \delta(\lambda) \right)^2 \cdot \frac{1}{2} \cdot \left( \frac{p_x}{\log \lambda} - \delta(\lambda) \right) \cdot \frac{1}{\log \lambda} \geq \\ &\geq \frac{(p_x)^3}{8(\log \lambda)^4} - \mathsf{negl}(\lambda) \;. \end{split}$$

{61}------------------------------------------------

Subsequently, in the following claim, we apply the properties of the coalescence function  $F(\cdot)$  and the security of the publicly samplable adaptive OWF family  ${\bf F}$  to prove that the probability that Valid, Uninf and E=E' happen is negligible.

<span id="page-61-0"></span>Claim 6  $\Pr[\mathsf{Valid} \wedge \mathsf{Uninf} \wedge (E = E')] = \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$ .

*Proof of claim:* Assume for the sake of contradiction that there is a non-negligible function  $\alpha(\cdot)$  s.t.

$$\Pr\left[\mathsf{Valid} \wedge \mathsf{Uninf} \wedge (E = E')\right] \ge \alpha(\lambda)$$
.

We show that we can construct a PPT algorithm  $\mathcal{B}$  that breaks the security publicly samplable adaptive one-way function family

$$\mathbf{F} = \{f_{\mathsf{tag}}: X_{\mathsf{tag}} \mapsto Y_{\mathsf{tag}}\}_{\mathsf{tag} \in \{0,1\}^{\frac{\lambda}{\log^2 \lambda}}}$$

with non-negligible probability  $\alpha'(\cdot)$ . Namely, there is a  $\mathsf{tag} \in \{0,1\}^{\frac{\lambda}{\log^2 \lambda}}$  s.t.

$$\Pr\left[d \leftarrow U_{\lambda/\log^2 \lambda} : \mathcal{B}^{\mathcal{O}(\mathsf{tag}, \cdot, \cdot)} \big(\mathsf{tag}, d\big) = f_{\mathsf{tag}}^{-1} \big(\mathsf{IM}(\mathsf{tag}, d)\big)\right] \geq \alpha'(\lambda) \; .$$

In particular, the algorithm  $\mathcal{B}$  is hardcoded with tag and  $(x, \mathcal{I}_{corr})$  and is given the code of CVZK.Ext,  $\mathcal{A}$ , the algorithm Sample guaranteed by the efficient samplability of  $F(\cdot)$ , and the knowledge extractor of InD. $\Pi$ , denoted by InD.Ext and guaranteed by the special soundness of InD. $\Pi$ . On input a randomly chosen value d, the algorithm  $\mathcal{B}$  executes the following steps:

- 1. It randomly chooses  $\ell_0 \in [n] \setminus \mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{corr}}$  setting  $\mathsf{tag}_{\ell_0} \leftarrow \mathsf{tag}$ .
- 2. It executes the steps of  $\mathsf{CVZK}.\mathsf{Ext}^{\mathcal{A}}(x,\mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{corr}})$  with the following modification for sampling C: for  $i \in [\log \lambda], i \neq i^*$  (where as before,  $i^*$  is the guess of  $\mathsf{CVZK}.\mathsf{Ext}$ ) for fixing the group  $G_i$ , it randomly picks  $d_i \leftarrow \{0,1\}^{\lambda/\log^2 \lambda}$ ; it then sets  $d_{i^*} := d$  and invokes  $\mathsf{Sample}(d_1, \ldots, d_{\log \lambda})$ , obtaining  $C \in \{0,1\}^n$ . If  $F(C) \neq (d_1, \ldots, d_{\log \lambda})$ , then it aborts.
- 3. Let  $\hat{C}$  and  $\hat{C}'$  be the adversarially tampered challenges. If  $d_{i^*}$  and  $d'_{i^*}$  derived by  $F(\hat{C})$  and  $F(\hat{C}')$  are not equal, then  $\mathcal{B}$  aborts. Note that if  $d_{i^*} = d'_{i^*}$ , then the adaptive OWF sampler on input  $d_{i^*}$  and any tag  $\mathsf{tag}_{\ell}$ ,  $\ell \in [n]$ , outputs the same statement  $x_{\ell,i^*}^* := \beta_{\ell,i^*} \leftarrow \mathsf{IM}(\mathsf{tag}_{\ell}, d_{i^*})$  in both runs.
- 4. Let  $R := (r_1, \ldots, r_n) = E \oplus \hat{C}$  and  $R' := (r'_1, \ldots, r'_n) = E' \oplus \hat{C}'$ . If  $r_{\ell_0} \neq r'_{\ell_0}$ , then  $\mathcal{B}$  aborts.
- 5. If CVZK.Verify $(x, A, \hat{C}, Z) = \text{CVZK.Verify}(x, A, \hat{C}', Z') = 1$  (i.e. Valid happens as well as  $d_{i^*} = d'_{i^*}$ , and  $r_{\ell_0} \neq r'_{\ell_0}$ ), then  $\mathcal{B}$
- (a). Computes the OWF challenge  $\beta \leftarrow \mathsf{IM}(\mathsf{tag},d)$  (i.e.  $\beta = x_{\ell_0,i^*}^*$ ).
- (b). Runs InD.Ext on input the two valid transcripts  $(\beta, a_{\ell_0, i^*}^*, r_{\ell_0}, z_{\ell_0, i^*}^*)$  and  $(\beta, a_{\ell_0, i^*}^*, r_{\ell_0}', z_{\ell_0, i^*}^*)$  and receives the output  $\alpha$  of InD.Ext. Then, CVZK.Ext returns  $\alpha$  as a preimage for  $\beta$ .

{62}------------------------------------------------

Clearly, by the special soundness of InD.II, the algorithm  $\mathcal{B}$  always returns a valid preimage when the event  $Valid \wedge (d_{i^*} = d'_{i^*}) \wedge (r_{\ell_0} \neq r'_{\ell_0})$  happens. Recall that Uninf is the event where in both runs, the output corresponding to the fixed  $i^*$ -th group  $G_{i^*}$  is uninfluenced. Thus, when Uninf happens  $d'_{i^*}$  and  $d'_{i^*}$  are always equal, and specifically equal to  $\mathcal{B}$ 's input, d, which means that  $\mathcal{B}$  breaks the adaptive one-wayness of  $\mathbf{F}$ .

We first argue that  $\mathcal{B}$  emulates  $\mathsf{CVZK}.\mathsf{Ext}^{\mathcal{A}}(x,\mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{corr}})$  with  $1 - \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$  probability. The latter holds by the efficient samplability of the coalescence function  $F(\cdot)$ , as proven in Theorem 1. Namely, since the adaptive OWF challenge d provided to  $\mathcal{B}$  is randomly chosen, the vector  $d_1, \ldots, d_{\log \lambda}$  that  $\mathcal{B}$  generates at step 2 is uniformly distributed over  $(\{0,1\}^{\lambda/\log^2 \lambda})^{\log \lambda}$ . Thus, with  $1 - \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$  probability  $\mathsf{Sample}(d_1, \ldots, d_{\log \lambda})$  outputs a value C s.t.  $F(C) = (d_1, \ldots, d_{\log \lambda})$ , so  $\mathcal{B}$  will not abort in this step. In addition, C is sampled statistically  $\mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$ -close to  $\mathbb{U}_n$ , i.e., according to the original  $\mathsf{CVZK}$  sampling that  $\mathsf{CVZK}.\mathsf{Ext}$  follows.

Let  $\operatorname{\mathsf{Ham}}(\cdot,\cdot)$  denote the Hamming distance between two strings of equal length. Recall that  $\hat{C},\hat{C}'$  derived by the tampering of at most  $t_2 \leq \frac{n^{1-\frac{1}{\gamma}}}{\log^3 n}$  bit positions in the two n-bit challenges  $C = (G_1,\ldots,G_{\lambda\log\lambda})$  and  $C' = (G'_1,\ldots,G'_{\lambda\log\lambda})$  respectively. Since C' is generated by fixing the bits in  $G'_{i^*}$  the same as  $G_{i^*}$  and randomly flipping all other positions, we have that C and C' differ at  $\frac{1}{2}\left(n-\frac{n}{\log\lambda}\right)$  bit positions on average. Hence, by the Chernoff bounds we have that

<span id="page-62-0"></span>
$$\Pr\left[\mathsf{Ham}(C, C') \ge n/3\right] \ge 1 - e^{-\frac{(\frac{n}{2} - \frac{n}{2\log\lambda})(\frac{n}{6} + \frac{n}{2\log\lambda})^2}{2}} = 1 - \mathsf{negl}(\lambda) \ . \tag{19}$$

Now suppose that C, C' differ in at least n/3 bit positions. In this case, since  $\frac{n^{1-\frac{1}{\gamma}}}{\log^3 n} = o(n)$  for any  $\gamma > 0$  and sufficiently large n, we deduce that whatever is the tampering strategy of  $\mathcal{A}$ , it is impossible that  $\mathcal{A}$  can produce two adversarial challenges  $\hat{C}, \hat{C}'$  that are "close". Specifically, by Eq. (19) and the fact that  $\mathcal{B}$  almost perfectly emulates  $\mathsf{CVZK}.\mathsf{Ext}^{\mathcal{A}}(x)$ , we have that with  $1 - \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$  probability and for sufficiently large n,

<span id="page-62-1"></span>
$$\operatorname{Ham}(\hat{C}, \hat{C}') \ge \frac{n}{3} - 2t_2 \ge \frac{n}{3} - 2\frac{n^{1 - \frac{1}{\gamma}}}{\log^3 n} \ge \frac{n}{4} . \tag{20}$$

Since  $\mathcal{B}$  chooses  $\ell_0$  uniformly at random, by Eq. (20) we get that

<span id="page-62-2"></span>
$$\Pr\left[\hat{c}_{\ell_0} \neq \hat{c}'_{\ell_0}\right] \ge \frac{1}{4} - \mathsf{negl}(\lambda) \ . \tag{21}$$

By our hypothesis, the fact that  $R = E \oplus \hat{C}$ ,  $R' = E' \oplus \hat{C}'$  and Eq. (21), we have that

{63}------------------------------------------------

<span id="page-63-0"></span>
$$\begin{split} &\Pr\left[\mathsf{Valid} \land \mathsf{Uninf} \land (E = E') \land (r_{\ell_0} \neq r'_{\ell_0})\right] = \\ &= \Pr\left[\mathsf{Valid} \land \mathsf{Uninf} \land (E = E')\right] \cdot \Pr\left[r_{\ell_0} \neq r'_{\ell_0} \mid \mathsf{Valid} \land \mathsf{Uninf} \land (E = E')\right] = \\ &= \Pr\left[\mathsf{Valid} \land \mathsf{Uninf} \land (E = E')\right] \cdot \Pr\left[\hat{c}_{\ell_0} \neq \hat{c}'_{\ell_0}\right] \geq \\ &\geq \frac{\alpha(\lambda)}{4} - \mathsf{negl}(\lambda) \;. \end{split} \tag{22}$$

By the description of  $\mathcal{B}$ , Eq. (22) provides a non-negligible lower bound for the success probability of  $\mathcal{B}$  in returning a valid preimage for the adaptive OWF challenge d. In detail, by setting  $\alpha'(\lambda) := \frac{\alpha(\lambda)}{4} - \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$ , we conclude that

$$\Pr\left[d \leftarrow U_{\lambda/\log^2 \lambda} : \mathcal{B}^{\mathcal{O}(\mathsf{tag},\cdot,\cdot)}\big(\mathsf{tag},d\big) = f_{\mathsf{tag}}^{-1}\big(\mathsf{IM}(\mathsf{tag},d)\big)\right] \ge \alpha'(\lambda) \;,$$

which leads to contradiction, due to the security of F. Thus, it must hold that

$$\Pr[\mathsf{Valid} \land \mathsf{Uninf} \land (E=E')] = \mathsf{negl}(\lambda) \; .$$
 
$$(End \; of \; proof \; of \; Claim) \; \dashv$$

Finally, the crowd verifiable validity of our construction follows directly by applying the Claims 5 and 6, where we get that

$$\begin{split} & \Pr\left[w^* \leftarrow \mathsf{CVZK}.\mathsf{Ext}^{\mathcal{A}}(x,\mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{corr}}) : (x,w^*) \in R_{\mathcal{L}}\right] \geq \\ & \geq \Pr\left[\mathsf{Valid} \land (E \neq E')\right] \geq \Pr\left[\mathsf{Valid} \land \mathsf{Uninf} \land (E \neq E')\right] = \\ & = \Pr\left[\mathsf{Valid} \land \mathsf{Uninf}\right] - \Pr\left[\mathsf{Valid} \land \mathsf{Uninf} \land (E \neq E')\right] \geq \\ & \geq \frac{(p_x)^3}{8(\log \lambda)^4} - \mathsf{negl}(\lambda) \;. \end{split}$$

Thus, if  $p_x$  is non-negligible, then CVZK.Ext extracts a valid witness with non-negligible probability as well.

**Crowd-verifiable ZK.** For some  $x, t_3, \mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{corr}}$ , s.t.  $|\mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{corr}}| \leq t_3$  let  $\mathcal{A}$  be a PPT adversary against the crowd-verifiable zero-knowledge of the CVZK protocol CVZK. $\Pi$  (presented in Section 4) that can tamper at most  $t_3$  verifier challenges. Namely, the adversary will win if it manages to distinguish the execution of the experiments  $\mathbf{Expt}^{\mathsf{CVZK}}_{(\mathsf{Ideal},\mathcal{A},\mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{corr}})}(1^{\lambda},x)$  and  $\mathbf{Expt}^{\mathsf{CVZK}}_{(\mathsf{Real},\mathcal{A},\mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{corr}})}(1^{\lambda},x)$  (as defined in Def. 3) for  $x \in \mathcal{L}$ .

Assume that  $\mathcal{A}$  has access to a helper  $\mathcal{H}_S$  from  $\mathbf{H}$ , where (i)  $\{\mathsf{tag}_\ell\}_{\ell\in\mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{corr}}}\subseteq S$  and (ii)  $\{\mathsf{tag}_\ell\}_{\ell\in[n]\setminus\mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{corr}}}\cap S=\emptyset$ . In addition, assume that  $\Sigma.\Pi$  and  $\mathsf{InD}.\Pi$  are sHVZK against PPT distinguishers that have access to the said helper  $\mathcal{H}_S$ . We start by explaining how to construct the CVZK simulator pair CVZK.Sim =  $(\mathsf{CVZK}.\mathsf{Sim}_1,\mathsf{CVZK}.\mathsf{Sim}_2^{\mathcal{H}_S})$  that will be used in  $\mathsf{Expt}^{\mathsf{CVZK}}_{(\mathsf{Ideal},\mathcal{A},\mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{corr}})}(1^\lambda,x)$ .

Recall that our CVZK construction works over any  $\Sigma$ -protocol and utilizes a fully input-delayed  $\Sigma$ -protocol so that the prover is convincing if it either knows a witness for the underlying  $\Sigma$ -protocol or knows a witness for the underlying

{64}------------------------------------------------

 $\mathsf{InD}.\Sigma$ -protocol. However, the witness for the  $\mathsf{InD}.\mathsf{Sim}$  proof is never known as it depends on statements generated using the verifiers' random challenges/bits that are received in the second step of the  $\mathsf{CVZK}$  protocol (thus the simulator from  $\mathsf{InD}.\Pi$  is used in the main  $\mathsf{CVZK}$  construction).

In the construction of the simulator there are two key ideas:

- 1. Since the witness w of the underlying  $\Sigma$ -protocol is no longer known, we replace the  $\Sigma$ -protocol prover by its corresponding simulator  $\Sigma$ . Sim. Thus, in  $\mathsf{CVZK}.\mathsf{Sim}_1$  we now use  $\Sigma.\mathsf{Sim}$  which when given the  $\Sigma$ -protocol challenges from  $\mathsf{CVZK}.\mathsf{Sim}_1$  it simulates the full transcript of the  $\Sigma$ -protocol which is stored in the state of the  $\mathsf{CVZK}$  simulator  $\mathsf{st}_{\mathsf{Sim}}$ .
- 2. The input-delayed proof is relevant to the verifiers' random coins. Since in the ZK definition we allow for corruption of verifiers we will treat the inputdelayed part of the CVZK simulator in a separate way for corrupted and non-corrupted verifiers. Namely, CVZK.Sim is identical to CVZK.Prv for all  $\ell \notin \mathcal{I}_{corr}$ , however it is no longer possible to use the InD. Sim simulator for the corrupted verifiers. The reason is that the corrupted verifiers are now allowed to flip their coins in the duration of the protocol (changing C to C' between executions of  $CVZK.Sim_1$  and  $CVZK.Sim_2$ ). As a result, InD.Sim cannot predict which coins to use in advance. To overcome this problem, for all  $\ell \in \mathcal{I}_{corr}$  we use the actual InD. II prover in the first step of the CVZK simulator, CVZK.Prv<sub>1</sub>, which is fine since in input-delayed proofs the statement does not have to be known in advance. Then, once the coins of all verifiers are received (corrupted or not) and the statements are set, we use the helper  $\mathcal{H}_S$ , as described in Fig. 1, to extract the witnesses (preimages) for the statements corresponding to the corrupted verifiers and conclude the input-delayed  $\Sigma$ -proof using the actual prover. Recall that  $\{\mathsf{tag}_{\ell}\}_{\ell \in \mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{corr}}} \subseteq S$ , so  $\mathcal{H}_S$  allows the extraction of witness for all statements corresponding to the corrupted verifiers.

Formally,  $CVZK.Sim = (CVZK.Sim_1, CVZK.Sim_2^{\mathcal{H}_S})$  runs as follows:

```
1. \mathsf{CVZK}.\mathsf{Sim}_1(x,\mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{corr}}): (where \mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{corr}} is the set of corrupted verifiers.)
```

```
- Pick random E \leftarrow \{0,1\}^n, and parse E = (e_1, \dots, e_{\log \lambda}).
```

- For  $\ell \notin \mathcal{I}_{corr}$ , choose a random bit  $c_{\ell}$ .
- For  $\ell \notin \mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{corr}}$ , set  $r_{\ell}$  as the XOR of  $c_{\ell}$  and the  $\ell$ -th bit of E.
- For  $i \in [\log \lambda]$ , run  $(a_i, z_i) \leftarrow \Sigma.\mathsf{Sim}(x, e_i)$ .
- For  $\ell \in [n]$ :
  - If  $\ell \notin \mathcal{I}_{corr}$ : run  $(a_{\ell}^*, \mathsf{st}_{\ell}^*) \leftarrow \mathsf{InD.Sim}_1(r_{\ell}, size)$ .
  - If  $\ell \in \mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{corr}}$ : for all  $j \in [\log \lambda]$  run  $(a_{\ell,j}^*, \mathsf{st}_{\ell,j}^*) \leftarrow \mathsf{InD}.\Sigma.\mathsf{Prv}_1(size)$ .
- Output  $A := (\{a_i\}_{i \in [\log \lambda]}, \{a_{\ell,j}^*\}_{\ell \in [n]}^{j \in [\log \lambda]}), \langle c_\ell \rangle_{\ell \in [n] \setminus \mathcal{I}_{corr}},$ and  $\mathsf{st}_{\mathsf{Sim}} := (E, \{z_i\}_{i \in [\log \lambda]}, \{\mathsf{st}_{\ell,j}^*\}_{\ell \in [n]}^{j \in [\log \lambda]}).$
- 2.  $\mathsf{CVZK}.\mathsf{Sim}_2^{\mathcal{H}_S}(\mathsf{st}_{\mathsf{Sim}}, C', \mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{corr}})$ : (where C' can differ from C only for the indices in  $\mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{corr}}$ .)
  - Parse  $\mathsf{st}_{\mathsf{Sim}} = (E, \{z_i\}_{i \in [\log \lambda]}, \{\mathsf{st}_{\ell}^*\}_{\ell \in [n]}).$
  - Set  $R' := (r'_1, \dots, r'_n) := E \oplus C'$ .

{65}------------------------------------------------

- Compute  $(d_1, \ldots, d_{\log \lambda}) \leftarrow F(C')$
- Compute  $(e_1, \ldots, e_{\log \lambda}) \leftarrow E$ .
- For  $\ell \in [n]$  and for  $j \in [\log \lambda]$ :
  - Run  $\beta_{\ell,j} \leftarrow \mathsf{IM}(\mathsf{tag}_{\ell}, d_j)$  and define the statement  $x_{\ell,j}^* = \beta_{\ell,j}$ .
  - If  $\ell \notin \mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{corr}} : z_{\ell,j}^* \leftarrow \mathsf{InD}.\mathsf{Sim}_2(\mathsf{st}_{\ell,j}^*, x_{\ell,j}^*)$ . Note that for  $\ell \notin \mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{corr}}$ , it holds that  $r_\ell' = r_\ell$ , therefore  $\mathsf{InD}.\mathsf{Sim}_2$  can complete the proof consistently.
  - If  $\ell \in \mathcal{I}_{corr}$ : make query  $(tag_{\ell}, \beta_{\ell,j})$  to  $\mathcal{H}_S$  to obtain an  $\alpha_{\ell,j}$  such that  $f_{tag_{\ell}}(\alpha_{\ell,j}) = \beta_{\ell,j}$ . Then, complete the proof by running  $z_{\ell}^* \leftarrow InD.Prv_2(x_{\ell,j}^*, \alpha_{\ell,j}, r_{\ell}', x_{\ell,j}^*, \mathsf{st}_{\ell,j}^*)$ .
- InD.Prv<sub>2</sub> $(x_{\ell,j}^*, \alpha_{\ell,j}, r_{\ell}', x_{\ell,j}^*, \mathsf{st}_{\ell,j}^*)$ .

   Output  $Z := (E, \{z_i\}_{i \in [\log \lambda]}, \{z_{\ell,j}^*\}_{\ell \in [n]}^{j \in [\log \lambda]})$ .

We will now show that the view generated by CVZK.Sim is computationally indistinguishable from the view of the adversary  $\mathcal A$  in an execution with the honest prover CVZK.Prv when CVZK.Sim is equipped with the helper oracle  $\mathcal H_S$ . Our proof is done through a sequence of hybrid experiments defined below.

*Hybrid*  $\theta$ : The view of  $\mathcal{A}$  is (x, A, C, Z) and is generated by CVZK.Sim, as in the ideal experiment  $\mathbf{Expt}^{\mathsf{CVZK}}_{(\mathsf{Ideal},t_3,\mathcal{A},\mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{corr}})}(1^\lambda,x)$ .

Hybrid 1: In this hybrid experiment, we consider an alternative CVZK simulator CVZK.Sim' = (CVZK.Sim'\_1, CVZK.Sim'\_2<sup>H\_S</sup>) that is given the witness w. Thus, for all  $i \in [\log \lambda]$ , CVZK.Sim'\_1 generates  $a_i$ 's using the actual Σ-protocol prover  $\Sigma$ .Prv<sub>1</sub>(x, w) and CVZK.Sim'\_2 generates  $z_i$ 's using  $\Sigma$ .Prv<sub>2</sub> $(\mathsf{st}_i, e_i)$ .

Hybrid 2: This final hybrid is the real game  $\mathbf{Expt}^{\mathsf{CVZK}}_{(\mathsf{Real},t_3,\mathcal{A},\mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{corr}})}(1^{\lambda},x,w)$ , i.e. the view of  $\mathcal{A}$  is generated by interacting with the actual prover  $\mathsf{CVZK}.\mathsf{Prv}$ .

- Indistinguishability between Hybrid 1 and Hybrid 0: By the sHVZK property against PPT distinguishers with access to  $\mathcal{H}_S$  of the underlying  $\Sigma$ -protocol  $\Sigma.\Pi$ , Hybrid 1 is computationally indistinguishable from Hybrid 0. Note that most common  $\Sigma$ -protocols achieve at least statistical (if not perfect) sHVZK, thus computational sHVZK w.r.t.  $\mathcal{H}_S$  is implied. Namely, after the execution of the updated CVZK.Sim'<sub>1</sub>, the view for  $\mathcal{A}$  will be  $A := (\{a_i\}_{i \in [\log \lambda]}, \{a_{\ell,j}^*\}_{\ell \in [n]}^{j \in [\log \lambda]})$ ,  $\langle c_\ell \rangle_{\ell \in [n] \setminus \mathcal{I}_{corr}}$  where  $\{a_{\ell,j}^*\}_{\ell \in [n]}^{j \in [\log \lambda]}$  and  $\langle c_\ell \rangle_{\ell \in [n] \setminus \mathcal{I}_{corr}}$  are computed exactly as in Hybrid 0 (i.e. interacting with CVZK.Sim<sub>1</sub>), while  $\{a_i\}_{i \in [\log \lambda]}$  are now computed using  $\Sigma$ .Prv<sub>1</sub>(x, w) instead. Similarly, after the execution of the updated CVZK.Sim'<sub>2</sub> the output  $Z := (E, \{z_i\}_{i \in [\log \lambda]}, \{z_{\ell,j}^*\}_{\ell \in [n]}^{j \in [\log \lambda]})$  will be identically distributed to Hybrid 0 except  $\{z_i\}_{i \in [\log \lambda]}$  that are now computed by  $\Sigma$ .Prv<sub>2</sub> instead.
