Indistinguishability follows because the sHVZK property of  $\Sigma$ -protocol  $\Sigma.\Pi$  is preserved under log  $\lambda$  parallel executions.

- Indistinguishability between Hybrid 2 and Hybrid 1: Next, we will argue that Hybrid 2 is indistinguishable from Hybrid 1 because of InD. $\Pi$  is sHVZK against PPT distinguishers with access to  $\mathcal{H}_S$ . Namely, the output of CVZK.Sim'<sub>1</sub> being  $A := (\{a_i\}_{i \in [\log \lambda]}, \{a_{\ell,j}^*\}_{\ell \in [n]}^{j \in [\log \lambda]}), \langle c_\ell \rangle_{\ell \in [n] \setminus \mathcal{I}_{corr}}$ , where  $\{a_i\}_{i \in [\log \lambda]}$  are identically distributed and  $\{a_{\ell,j}^*\}_{\ell \in [n]}^{j \in [\log \lambda]}$  are parts of the input-delayed proof. In Hybrid 2,  $\{a_{\ell,j}^*\}_{\ell \in [n]}^{j \in [\log \lambda]}$  are generated by using InD.Sim<sub>1</sub>; in Hybrid 1, for all

{66}------------------------------------------------

 $\ell \in \mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{corr}}$ , the corresponding  $\{a_{\ell,j}^*\}_{\ell \in [n]}^{j \in [\log \lambda]}$  are computed in the exact same way (thus perfectly indistinguishable), while for  $\ell \not\in \mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{corr}}$ ,  $\{a_{\ell,j}^*\}_{\ell \in [n]}^{j \in [\log \lambda]}$  are computed by  $\mathsf{InD.Prv}_1$ . Note that  $\mathsf{InD.Prv}_1$  does not need to receive any extra input compared to  $\mathsf{InD.Sim}_1$ .

We now move to argue that the view of  $\mathcal{A}$  is indistinguishable when running  $\mathsf{CVZK}.\mathsf{Sim}_2'$  (Hybrid 1) and  $\mathsf{CVZK}.\mathsf{Prv}_2$  (Hybrid 2). Let  $Z := (E, \{z_i\}_{i \in [\log \lambda]}, \{z_{\ell,j}^*\}_{\ell \in [n]}^{j \in [\log \lambda]})$  be the output of  $\mathsf{CVZK}.\mathsf{Sim}_2'$ . Observe that E is chosen uniformly at random, i.e. identically to real experiment where  $\mathsf{CVZK}.\mathsf{Prv}_2$  outputs the XOR of a random string R chosen by  $\mathsf{CVZK}.\mathsf{Prv}_1$  with the adversarial challenge. In addition,  $\{z_i\}_{i \in [\log \lambda]}$  are computed the exact same way. Thus, it remains to argue about the indistinguishability of  $\{z_{\ell,j}^*\}_{\ell \in [n]}^{j \in [\log \lambda]}$ . First, note that for all honest verifiers, i.e.  $\ell \notin \mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{corr}}$ ,  $\mathsf{CVZK}.\mathsf{Sim}_2'$  is identical to the real prover thus the produced  $z_\ell^*$ 's are perfectly indistinguishable. Let us finally discuss the corrupted verifiers, i.e.  $\ell \in \mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{corr}}$ . In Hybrid 1, we use  $\mathsf{InD}.\mathsf{Prv}_2$  to produce the corresponding  $\{z_{\ell,j}^*\}_{\ell \in [n]}^{j \in [\log \lambda]}$ , while in the real game  $\mathsf{InD}.\mathsf{Sim}_2$  is used. In order for  $\mathsf{InD}.\mathsf{Prv}_2$  to be able to output a valid input-delayed  $\mathcal{E}$  proof it needs the actual witness to the corresponding statements being proved. As mentioned above, this is possible due to the use of  $\mathcal{H}_S$ . Indistinguishability follows due to the sHVZK property of the  $\mathcal{E}$ -protocol  $\mathsf{InD}.\mathcal{H}$  for every statement  $x_{\ell,j}^*$ ,  $\ell \in [n]$ ,  $j \in [\log \lambda]$ .

By the transitive property of indistinguishability of Hybrid  $0 \to \text{Hybrid } 1 \to \text{Hybrid } 2$ , we conclude that  $\mathbf{Expt}^{\mathsf{CVZK}}_{(\mathsf{Ideal},t_3,\mathcal{A},\mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{corr}})}(1^{\lambda},x)$  and  $\mathbf{Expt}^{\mathsf{CVZK}}_{(\mathsf{Real},t_3,\mathcal{A},\mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{corr}})}(1^{\lambda},x,w)$  are indistinguishable. Hence, our construction is crowd-verifiable ZK against PPT adversaries with oracle access to  $\mathcal{H}_S$ .

# <span id="page-66-0"></span>D Comments on the strength of the VMPC Security Model

We elaborate on the discussion intitiated in Section 5.

The necessity of trusting at least one server for privacy. Suppose that all servers  $S_1, \ldots, S_k$  are corrupted, yet the private input  $x_\ell$  is not leaked to the simulator Sim. In the real world, corrupting all servers enables the real world adversary  $\mathcal{A}$  to completely run the **Compute** protocol. Since the users do not engage simultaneously, the environment can schedule  $U_\ell$  to participate first. The correctness of the VMPC scheme  $\Pi$  implies that by receiving the data from  $U_\ell$ ,  $\mathcal{A}$  can run **Compute** as if  $U_\ell$  was the only participant and learn the evaluation of f for input (abstain, ..., abstain, ..., abstain). Therefore, it can infer information for  $x_\ell$  that, in general, Sim can not obtain, for most functions of interest, as it only receives the evaluation of f on the entire input vector.

The necessity of trusting the clients for privacy. Since  $U_{\ell}$  has no other communication channel with  $S_1, \ldots, S_k$  other than via  $C_{\ell}$ , and also lacks access to a trusted setup that could be used to provide cryptographic key information, it cannot prevented that  $C_{\ell}$  will infer  $x_{\ell}$ . To see this, consider the view of  $C_{\ell}$  for two different values of  $x_{\ell}$  that result in different outputs with respect to

{67}------------------------------------------------

f; in such case,  $C_{\ell}$  can mount the following attack to distinguish between the two possible values of  $x_{\ell}$ : simulate  $S_1, \ldots, S_k$ , for fixed values for all users other than  $U_{\ell}$ , and exploit the correctness of the VMPC evaluation to compute f and hence infer  $x_{\ell}$ . Thus, if  $C_{\ell} \in L_{\text{corr}}$  but the private input  $x_{\ell}$  is not leaked to the simulator Sim, then the ideal and the real world can be distinguished.

The necessity of trusting the verifier for end-to-end verifiability. Clearly, in the ideal world setting  $\mathcal{F}_{\mathsf{vmpc}}^{f,R}(\mathcal{P})$ , plays the role of a trusted verifier which output depends on the fixed relation R that sets the rules of successful verification. However, in the real world, if  $\mathcal{A}$  corrupts the verifier, then it can recommend arbitrary verdicts regardless if the intended evaluation y and the value y' that  $\mathcal{A}$  returns is in relation R or not. Recall that in our setting, users consult the verifier for end-to-end verifiability since they are computationally limited.

# <span id="page-67-0"></span>E Supplementary Material for Section 6

#### E.1 The intuition behind Theorem 3

According to the statement of Theorem 3, if R is not a  $\delta$ -spreading relation over  $\operatorname{Img}[f]$ , then for given values of  $\kappa$ ,  $\delta$ , there is a lower bound  $\min\{2^{-\kappa\delta} - \epsilon(\lambda), \gamma(\lambda)\}$  on the best possible error in the VMPC computation of f, if the latter is feasible. For simplicity, ignoring the negligible term  $\epsilon(\lambda)$  and considering the nonnegligible value  $\gamma(\lambda)$  sufficiently large, in the rest of this discussion we can assume that the lower bound for the VMPC security error is practically  $2^{-\kappa\delta}$ , when  $\kappa > 0$ . Given this assumption, we make the following interesting observations.

Reaching the optimal expectation in VMPC feasibility. Although not  $\delta$ -spreading, it can be the case (yet not necessarily) that R is  $\delta'$ -spreading over  $\mathsf{Img}[f]$ , obviously for some value  $\delta' < \delta$ . Let  $\mathsf{Spr}_R \subseteq \mathbb{R}_{\geq 0}$  be the set non-negative values  $\delta'$  s.t. R is  $\delta'$ -spreading over  $\mathsf{Img}[f]$ . Note that we can theoritically consider cases where  $\mathsf{Spr}_R = \emptyset$ , yet we choose to restrict to the interesting scenario where  $\mathsf{Spr}_R$  contains at least one value. Hence, given that  $\mathsf{Spr}_R$  is non-empty, there is a supremum value  $\delta^* := \sup\{\mathsf{Spr}_R\}$  s.t. R is  $\delta^*$ -spreading over  $\mathsf{Img}[f]$ . By the definition of  $\delta^*$  and Theorem 3, the value  $2^{-\kappa\delta^*}$  sets the best possible error (ignoring negligible cryptographic error terms) that one can expect for the VMPC feasibility of f w.r.t. R. As it is shown in Theorem 4, our VMPC construction meets this essentially optimal expectation for the special case where  $\kappa=1$ .

The necessity for non-deterministic human behavior. The above observation reveals a crucial requirement on the "behavior" of the human users when they engage in the VMPC execution. Namely, if the users engage in a deterministic way, i.e.  $\kappa = 0$ , then VMPC feasibility with a reasonably small error is not possible whatever the value of  $\delta^{*9}$ . The latter fact captures the intuition that against an all-malicious environment, the humans must behave in a way

<span id="page-67-1"></span><sup>&</sup>lt;sup>9</sup> In particular, since  $\delta^* \leq n < +\infty$ , we have that  $2^{-\kappa\delta^*} = 1$ , so the best possible VMPC security error that we can expect is the non-negligible value  $\gamma(\lambda)$ .

{68}------------------------------------------------

that is somehow "unpredictable" in the view of the adversary. This is a conclusion that not only formally expresses, but also extends to the general MPC setting the intuition behind the design of all state-of-the-art end-to-end verifiable e-voting systems. There, the (human) voters are encouraged to engage in a "cast-or-audit" ballot verification mechanism which can be seen as a form of cut-and-choose proofs.

## E.2 Proof of Theorem 3

*Proof (sketch).* Assume that condition (1) of Theorem 3 does not hold. Then, by Definition 7, there exist  $\mathbf{x} = (x_1, \dots, x_n), \mathbf{x}' = (x'_1, \dots, x'_n) \in X^n$  s.t.

$$\operatorname{Dcr}_n(\mathbf{x}, \mathbf{x}') \leq \delta \operatorname{AND} \neg R(f(\mathbf{x}), f(\mathbf{x}'))$$
.

Let  $\Pi^{\mathcal{G}_{\mathsf{BB}},\mathcal{F}_{\mathsf{sc}}}$  be a VMPC scheme operating with the parties in  $\mathcal{P}$  with user min entropy  $\kappa$  that achieves correctness and a helper functionality  $\mathcal{H}$ . We will show that condition (2) holds. Namely, there is a negligible function  $\epsilon$  and a nonnegligible function  $\gamma$  such that  $\Pi^{\mathcal{G}_{\mathsf{BB}},\mathcal{F}_{\mathsf{sc}}}$  can not  $\mathcal{H}$ -EUC realize  $\mathcal{F}^{f,R}_{\mathsf{vmpc}}(\mathcal{P})$  with error less than  $\min\{2^{-\kappa\delta} - \epsilon(\lambda), 2^{-\kappa}\gamma(\lambda)\}$ .

We denote by y, y' the evaluations  $f(\mathbf{x}), f(\mathbf{x}')$ . respectively. We also denote by  $\tau, \tau'$  the transcript committed on the BB when execution is run on inputs  $\mathbf{x}, \mathbf{x}'$ , respectively

Since  $\operatorname{Dcr}_n X^n(\mathbf{x}, \mathbf{x}') \leq \delta$ , there is a subset of indices  $\mathcal{I}_{\operatorname{diff}} \in [n]$  of size  $\delta$  that contains all positions where components of the vectors  $\mathbf{x}, \mathbf{x}'$  differ. Formally, we have that

$$|\mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{diff}}| \leq \delta \text{ and } \forall \ell \in [n] \setminus \mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{diff}} : x_{\ell} = x'_{\ell}$$

Let  $\alpha_{\ell}, \alpha'_{\ell}$  be the individual audit data of user  $U_{\ell}$  when engaging on input  $x_{\ell}$ . We distinguish the following cases:

Case 1: The random variables  $\langle (\alpha_1, \ldots, \alpha_n), \tau' \rangle$  and  $\langle (\alpha'_1, \ldots, \alpha'_n), \tau' \rangle$  are indistinguishable.

This case refers to the concept of delegatable verification. Namely, the individual audit data should not leak information about the user's input to the verifier. This feature is desirable for privacy, but it can be exploited by an adversary that manages to guess the users' coins to break end-to-end verifiability as described below.

We construct a real world adversary  $\mathcal{A}_{e2e}$  against the end-to-end verifiability of  $\Pi^{\mathcal{G}_{BB},\mathcal{F}_{sc}}$ , that executes the following steps:

- 1. It manages the VMPC execution by corrupting all the servers and the users' clients.
- 2. For every  $\ell \in \mathcal{I}_{diff}$ , it guesses the randomness,  $r_{\ell}$ , of user  $U_{\ell}$  as the string  $\tilde{r}_{\ell}$ .
- 3. It runs the VMPC execution normally except the following modification: for every  $\ell \in \mathcal{I}_{diff}$ , regardless of the input of  $U_{\ell}$ , it interacts with  $U_{\ell}$  as if the latter was provided with input  $x'_{\ell}$  and generated internal randomness  $\tilde{r}_{\ell}$ .
- 4. It runs the **Compute** protocol honestly on input  $\mathbf{x}'$  by running all servers.

{69}------------------------------------------------

5. It allows the verifier V to interact normally and send the message (y', v) to each  $U_{\ell}$ ,  $\ell \in [n]$ .

Let  $G_{\text{e2e}}$  be the event that for every  $\ell \in \mathcal{I}_{\text{diff}}$ ,  $\mathcal{A}_{\text{e2e}}$  guesses the coins of the users  $U_{\ell}$ ,  $\ell \in \mathcal{I}_{\text{diff}}$ . By independency of the users' interaction and the fact that (a)  $|\mathcal{I}_{\text{diff}}| \leq \delta$  and (b) the user min entropy in  $\Pi$  is  $\kappa$ , we have that

<span id="page-69-0"></span>
$$\Pr[G_{\mathsf{e2e}}] = \prod_{\ell \in \mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{diff}}} \Pr\left[\mathcal{A}_{\mathsf{e2e}} \text{ guesses } \tilde{r}_{\ell} = r_{\ell}\right] \ge 2^{-\kappa\delta} . \tag{23}$$

Assume that  $G_{\text{e2e}}$  occurs. Then, by corrupting all servers and clients,  $\mathcal{A}_{\text{e2e}}$  can totally control the users' interaction and the individual audit data that they provide to the verifier V. In particular, V perform verification given (i) a collection of individual audit data  $(\alpha_1, \ldots, \alpha_n)$  corresponding to  $\mathbf{x}$  and (ii) a BB transcript  $\tau'$  that corresponds to  $\mathbf{x}'$ .

Observe that in an honest VMPC execution, where the individual audit data  $(\alpha'_1,\ldots,\alpha'_n)$  corresponding to  $\mathbf{x}',V$  will send the value 1 to all users if the execution was run honestly. Besides, by the assumption for Case 1,  $\langle (\alpha_1,\ldots,\alpha_n),\tau'\rangle$  and  $\langle (\alpha'_1,\ldots,\alpha'_n),\tau'\rangle$  are indistinguishable by any PPT algorithm, hence also by V. Therefore, according to description of  $\mathcal{A}_{\text{e2e}}$ , if for every  $\ell \in \mathcal{I}_{\text{diff}}$ : (i)  $U_\ell$  has input  $x_\ell$  and (ii)  $\mathcal{A}_{\text{e2e}}$  guesses the randomness of  $U_\ell$  (i.e.  $\tilde{r}_\ell = r_\ell$ ), then the PPT verifier V can not distinguish between  $\alpha'_\ell$  and the honest execution data  $\alpha_\ell$ , where it would be accepting verification. The latter implies that on input  $\langle (\alpha_1,\ldots,\alpha_n),\tau'\rangle$ , the verifier V will send  $(y',v_\ell=1)$  to each  $U_\ell$ ,  $\ell\in[n]$  with probability at least  $1-\mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$ .where  $\langle \epsilon_\ell(\cdot)\rangle_{\ell\in\mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{diff}}}$  are negligible functions. Given the above, consider the following environment that takes advantage of  $\mathcal{A}_{\mathsf{e2e}}$ 's strategy:

 $-\mathcal{Z}_{y',\text{Ver}}$ : selects  $\mathbf{x} \in X^n$  and sends the message (Cast,  $\text{sid}, x_\ell$ ) as input to each user  $U_\ell, \ell \in [n]$ . It instructs the full corruption of the servers and the clients. It outputs 1 iff all users return the message (Result, sid, y', 1).

By the above analysis and Eq. (23), in the real world setting, we have that for any helper  ${\cal H}$ 

$$\Pr\left[\mathsf{EXEC}_{\mathcal{A}_{\mathsf{e2e}},\mathcal{Z}_{y',\mathsf{Ver}},\mathcal{H}}^{\mathcal{G}_{\mathsf{BB}},\mathcal{F}_{\mathsf{sc}}}(\lambda) = 1\right] \geq$$

$$\geq \Pr[G_{\mathsf{e2e}}] \cdot \Pr\left[\mathsf{EXEC}_{\mathcal{A}_{\mathsf{e2e}},\mathcal{Z}_{y',\mathsf{Ver}},\mathcal{H}}^{\mathcal{G}_{\mathsf{BB}},\mathcal{F}_{\mathsf{sc}}}(\lambda) = 1 \mid G_{\mathsf{e2e}}\right] \geq$$

$$\geq 2^{-\kappa\delta} \cdot \left(1 - \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)\right) = 2^{-\kappa\delta} - \mathsf{negl}(\lambda). \tag{24}$$

<span id="page-69-1"></span>On the other hand, in the ideal world, if the environment's input vector is  $\mathbf{x}$ , then any ideal simulator  $\mathsf{Sim}$  with access to  $\mathcal{H}$  can follow either two of the possible strategies:

- (S.1). Neglect the strategy of  $\mathcal{A}_{e2e}$  and send the message (Verify\_response, sid,  $U_{\ell}, y'', \tilde{v}$ ) to  $\mathcal{F}_{vmpc}^{f,R}(\mathcal{P})$ , for some  $\ell \in [n]$ , where  $y'' \neq y'$  and  $\tilde{v} \in \{0,1\}$ .
- (S.2). Simulate an execution respecting  $\mathcal{A}_{e2e}$ 's steps and send (VERIFY\_RESPONSE, sid,  $U_{\ell}, y', \tilde{v}$ ) to  $\mathcal{F}_{vmpc}^{f,R}(\mathcal{P})$  for all  $\ell \in [n]$ .

{70}------------------------------------------------

Whenever the simulator follows (S.1), the environment  $\mathcal{Z}_{y',\mathsf{Ver}}$  will never output 1. Indeed, note that in any case,  $\mathcal{F}^{f,R}_{\mathsf{vmpc}}(\mathcal{P})$  will send either (Result, sid, y'',1) or (Result, sid, y'',0) to  $U_\ell$ , where  $y'' \neq y'$ . Moreover, whenever the simulator follows (S.2),  $\mathcal{Z}_{y',\mathsf{Ver}}$  will never output 1 as by assumption  $\neg R(y,y')$  so  $\mathcal{F}^{f,R}_{\mathsf{vmpc}}(\mathcal{P})$  will send (Result, sid, y',0) to all users. We conclude that

$$\Pr\left[\mathsf{EXEC}^{\mathcal{F}^{f,R}_{\mathsf{vmpc}}(\mathcal{P}),\mathcal{G}_{\mathsf{BB}}}_{\mathsf{Sim},\mathcal{Z}_{y'},\mathsf{Ver}}(\lambda) = 1\right] = 0 \;.$$

Therefore, by Eq. (24), therefore there exists a negligible function  $\epsilon(\lambda)$  s.t.  $\mathcal{Z}_{y',\text{Ver}}$  has distinguishing advantage at least

$$\begin{split} \Big| \Pr \big[ \mathsf{EXEC}_{\mathsf{Sim}, \mathcal{Z}_{y', \mathsf{Ver}}, \mathcal{H}}^{\mathcal{F}_{\mathsf{vmpc}}^{f, R}(\mathcal{P}), \mathcal{G}_{\mathsf{BB}}}(\lambda) = 1 \big] - \\ - \Pr \big[ \mathsf{EXEC}_{\mathcal{A}_{\mathsf{e2e}}, \mathcal{Z}_{y', \mathsf{Ver}}, \mathcal{H}}^{\mathcal{G}_{\mathsf{BB}}, \mathcal{F}_{\mathsf{sc}}}(\lambda) = 1 \big] \geq 2^{-\kappa \delta} - \epsilon(\lambda) \; . \end{split}$$

Case 2: There exists a PPT algorithm  $\mathcal{D}^*$  that distinguishes  $\langle (\alpha_1, \ldots, \alpha_n), \tau' \rangle$  and  $\langle (\alpha'_1, \ldots, \alpha'_n), \tau' \rangle$  with non-negligible advantage  $\beta(\lambda)$ .

Since all users derive their individual audit data independently and  $\alpha_{\ell}$  is identically distributed to  $\alpha'_{\ell}$  for  $\ell \notin \mathcal{I}_{\text{diff}}$ , we can assume that  $\mathcal{D}^*$  distinguishes  $(\alpha_{\ell})_{\ell \in \mathcal{I}_{\text{diff}}}$  and  $(\alpha'_{\ell})_{\ell \in \mathcal{I}_{\text{diff}}}$  with non-negligible advantage  $\beta(\lambda)$ . W.l.o.g, assume that

$$\Pr\left[\mathcal{D}^*(\alpha_\ell)_{\ell\in\mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{diff}}}\right) = 1\right] - \Pr\left[\mathcal{D}^*(\alpha'_\ell)_{\ell\in\mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{diff}}}\right) = 0\right] \ge \beta(\lambda) \ .$$

For  $|\mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{diff}}| \leq \delta \leq \frac{n}{2}$ , we construct the environment  $\mathcal{Z}^*$  that operates as follows:

- 1. It selects randomly a bit  $b \in \{0, 1\}$ .
- 2. If b = 0, it sets the users' input vector  $\tilde{\mathbf{x}} = (\tilde{x}_1, \dots, \tilde{x}_n)$  as

$$\tilde{x}_{\ell} := \begin{cases} x_{\ell}, & \text{if } 1 \leq \ell \leq |\mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{diff}}| \\ x'_{\ell}, & \text{if } |\mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{diff}}| + 1 \leq \ell \leq 2|\mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{diff}}| \\ \mathsf{abstain}, & \text{otherwise} \end{cases}$$

- 3. If b = 1, it sets the users' input vector  $\tilde{\mathbf{x}} = (\tilde{x}_1, \dots, \tilde{x}_n)$  as above by swapping the role of  $x_\ell$  and  $x'_\ell$ .
- 4. It instructs the corruption of V and schedules the rest of the execution normally.
- 5. It obtains the audit data  $(\tilde{\alpha}_1, \dots, \tilde{\alpha}_{|\mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{diff}}|})$  from V and provides them as input to  $\mathcal{D}^*$ .
- 6. If  $\mathcal{D}^*$  guesses b, then it returns 1. Otherwise, it returns a random bit.

Assume now a real world execution scheduled by  $\mathcal{Z}^*$  which is dummy except from the fact that it corrupts V in order to forward the individual audit data to  $\mathcal{Z}^*$ . Then, clearly, it holds that

{71}------------------------------------------------

<span id="page-71-0"></span>
$$\Pr\left[\mathsf{EXEC}_{\mathcal{A}^*,\mathcal{Z}^*,\mathcal{H}}^{\mathcal{P}\mathsf{BB},\mathcal{F}\mathsf{sc}}(\lambda) = 1\right] = \frac{1}{2} \cdot \Pr\left[\mathsf{EXEC}_{\mathcal{A}^*,\mathcal{Z}^*,\mathcal{H}}^{\mathcal{P}\mathsf{BB},\mathcal{F}\mathsf{sc}}(\lambda) = 1 \mid b = 1\right] + \frac{1}{2} \cdot \Pr\left[\mathsf{EXEC}_{\mathcal{A}^*,\mathcal{Z}^*,\mathcal{H}}^{\mathcal{P}\mathsf{BB},\mathcal{F}\mathsf{sc}}(\lambda) = 1 \mid b = 0\right] = \frac{1}{2} \cdot \left(\Pr\left[\mathcal{D}^*(\alpha_{\ell})_{\ell \in \mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{diff}}}\right) = 1 \mid b = 1\right] \cdot 1 + \Pr\left[\mathcal{D}^*(\alpha_{\ell})_{\ell \in \mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{diff}}}\right) = 0 \mid b = 1\right] \cdot \frac{1}{2}\right) + \frac{1}{2} \cdot \left(\Pr\left[\mathcal{D}^*(\alpha_{\ell}')_{\ell \in \mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{diff}}}\right) = 0 \mid b = 0\right] \cdot 1 + \Pr\left[\mathcal{D}^*(\alpha_{\ell}')_{\ell \in \mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{diff}}}\right) = 1 \mid b = 0\right] \cdot \frac{1}{2}\right) + \frac{1}{2} \cdot \left(\Pr\left[\mathcal{D}^*(\alpha_{\ell}')_{\ell \in \mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{diff}}}\right) = 1 \mid b = 0\right] \cdot \frac{1}{2}\right) + \frac{1}{2} \cdot \left(\Pr\left[\mathcal{D}^*(\alpha_{\ell})_{\ell \in \mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{diff}}}\right) = 1\right] - \Pr\left[\mathcal{D}^*(\alpha_{\ell}')_{\ell \in \mathcal{I}_{\mathsf{diff}}}\right) = 0\right]\right) \geq \frac{1}{2} + \frac{\beta(\lambda)}{2}.$$

On the other hand, in the ideal world, an arbitrary simulator Sim with oracle access to  $\mathcal{H}$  running under the instructions of  $\mathcal{Z}^*$ , will send the message (VERIFY\_RESPONSE, sid,  $U_{\ell^*}, y^*, \tilde{v}$ ) to  $\mathcal{F}^{f,R}_{\mathsf{vmpc}}(\mathcal{P})$  without obtaining  $x_{\ell^*}$ , since the users' clients are not corrupted. By construction of  $\mathcal{Z}^*$ ,  $\mathcal{F}^{f,R}_{\mathsf{vmpc}}(\mathcal{P})$  will always output the value  $\tilde{y} = f(\tilde{\mathbf{x}})$  for any value of  $b \in \{0,1\}$ , given that f is a symmetric function. Therefore, Sim's steps will be independent of b and it holds that

<span id="page-71-1"></span>
$$\Pr\left[\mathsf{EXEC}_{\mathsf{Sim},\mathcal{Z}^*,\mathcal{H}}^{\mathcal{F}^{f,R}(\mathcal{P}),\mathcal{G}_{\mathsf{BB}}}(\lambda) = 1\right] = \frac{1}{2}$$
 (26)

By Eq. (25) and (26), we have that the distinguishing advantage of  $\mathcal{Z}^*$  between the ideal-world and the real-world execution is  $\frac{\beta(\lambda)}{2}$ , which is non-negligible.

Completing the proof. Combining the results from Cases 1 and 2 and setting  $\gamma(\lambda) = \frac{\beta(\lambda)}{2}$ , we get that for any VMPC scheme  $\Pi$  and any helper  $\mathcal{H}$ , it holds that there is a real-world adversary ( $\mathcal{A}_{e2e}$  or  $\mathcal{A}^*$ ) and an environment ( $\mathcal{Z}_{y'_{\mathsf{Ver}}}$  or  $\mathcal{Z}^*$ ) such that for every simulator Sim, it holds that

$$\begin{split} & \left| \Pr \big[ \mathsf{EXEC}^{\mathcal{F}^{f,R}_{\mathsf{vmpc}}(\mathcal{P}),\mathcal{G}_{\mathsf{BB}}}_{\mathsf{Sim},\mathcal{Z},\mathcal{H}}(\lambda) = 1 \big] - \\ & - \Pr \left[ \mathsf{EXEC}^{\mathcal{P},\Pi^{\mathcal{G}_{\mathsf{BB}},\mathcal{F}_{\mathsf{sc}}}}_{\mathcal{A}^*,\mathcal{Z}^*,\mathcal{H}}(\lambda) = 1 \right] \right| \geq \min \{ 2^{-\kappa\delta} - \epsilon(\lambda), \gamma(\lambda) \}. \end{split}$$

Namely,  $\Pi$  does not  $\mathcal{H}$ -realize  $\mathcal{F}_{\mathsf{vmpc}}^{f,R}(\mathcal{P})$  with error less than  $\min\{2^{-\kappa\delta} - \epsilon(\lambda), \gamma(\lambda)\}$ .

{72}------------------------------------------------

# F Supplementary Material for VMPC Construction

# <span id="page-72-0"></span>F.1 Dual-mode Homomorphic Commitments

To enable VMPC, similar as [\[4\]](#page-35-3), the MPC servers need to commit to every shared value to the bulletin board. The commitment scheme should be additively homomorphic (for both message and randomness); however, commitment schemes like lifted ElGamal (presented above) or Pedersen commitment are not sufficient in our setting. Given that in VMPC the commitment key is generated by the servers, Pedersen commitment becomes not binding when all the servers are malicious, while ElGamal commitment does not allow the simulator to equivocate. To overcome these problems, we make use of dual-mode homomorphic commitments which allow for two ways to choose the commitment key such that the commitment is either perfectly binding or equivocal. In the following, we adopt and modify the dual-mode commitment definition proposed by [\[52\]](#page-37-25) to against helger-aided PPT adversaries.

Definition 12. A dual-mode commitment scheme is a tuple of PPT algorithms DC.Gen, DC.Com, DC.Sim such that

- 1. DC.Gen(1<sup>λ</sup> ) outputs a commitment key denoted ck.
- 2. When ck ← DC.Gen(1<sup>λ</sup> ) and m ∈ { 0, 1 } λ the algorithm DC.Comck(m; r) with a random r is a non-interactive perfectly-binding commitment scheme with de-commitment algorithm DC.Open and de-commitment verification algorithm DC.Ver. We require that
  - DC.Verck(DC.Comck(m; r), DC.Openck(m; r)) = m except with negligible probability.
- 3. For every PPT adversary A aided by H<sup>S</sup> and every polynomial p(·), the output of the following two experiments is computationally indistinguishable:

```
ExptReal
      Com,A(1λ
                )
- ck ← DC.Gen(1λ
                      )
- For i = 1, . . . , p(λ):
       1. mi ← A(ck, c, r)
       2. ri ← { 0, 1 }
                       poly(1λ)
       3. ci = DC.Comck(mi
                               ; ri)
        4. Set c = c1, . . . , ci and r =
   r1, . . . , ri
- Output
   A(cki
         , m1, r1, . . . , mp(λ)
                              , rp(λ))
```

{73}------------------------------------------------

```
ExptSimulation
      DC.Sim (1λ
                    )
- ck ← DC.Gen(1λ
                     )
- For i = 1, . . . , p(λ):
      1. ci = DC.Sim
      2. mi ← A(ck, c, r)
      3. ri ← DC.Sim(mi)
       4. Set c = c1, . . . , ci and r =
   r1, . . . , ri
- Output
   A(cki
         , m1, r1, . . . , mp(λ)
                             , rp(λ))
```

Our construction is built on top of the perfectly binding commitment as proposed in [C.2.](#page-48-0) The resulting commitment should be either perfectly binding or perfectly equivocatable, depending on the commitment key. Consistently with our setting, we require that the servers jointly generate the commitment key and post it in the GBB. Then, commitment executions may run among servers or between servers and clients. We denote by CS<sup>F</sup> = (CSF.Gen, CSF.Com, CSF.Ver) the perfectly binding commitment that is secure against helper-aided PPT adversaries. Our dual mode commitment scheme DC works as follows:

```
DC.Gen(1λ
          ):
 – Generate ck ← CSF.Gen(1λ
                              );
 – Commit c ← CSF.Com(1; t) with fresh randomness t ← (Zq)
                                                               k
                                                                 ;
 – Output the commitment key as ck∗
                                       := (ck, c).
DC.Comck∗ (m; r):
 – To commit a message m ∈ Zq, return commitment c
                                                       ∗ ← c
                                                            m ·CSF.Com(0; r).
DC.Openck(c, m, r):
 – To open commitment c, return (m, r).
DC.Verck(c, m, r):
 – Output 1 iff c
                 ∗ = c
                      m · CSF.Com(0; r).
```

By notation c <sup>m</sup>, we meant element-wisely power each element of c of m. Similarly, c<sup>1</sup> · c<sup>2</sup> stands for element-wise product (a.k.a. Hadamard product).

Clearly, the commitment scheme is perfect binding when c is a commitment of a non-zero message, say 1 w.r.t. CS<sup>F</sup> as proposed in [C.2.](#page-48-0) However, if we set the commitment key with c ← CS<sup>F</sup>.Com(0; t), then it is perfectly equivocatable, given the trapdoor information td := t. More specifically, we can open DC.Comck(m; r) to any given m<sup>0</sup> ∈ Z<sup>q</sup> and r <sup>0</sup> = t(m − m<sup>0</sup> ) +r. During our proof, the simulator will switch these two types of commitment keys via rewinding.

{74}------------------------------------------------

## <span id="page-74-0"></span>F.2 A $\Sigma$ -Protocol for Beaver Triples

We now propose a  $\Sigma$ -protocol for proving A, B, C commits to a, b, c and ab = c. The protocol is depicted in Fig. 8.

```
Common input: ck. Statement: A := DC.Com_{ck}(a; \mathbf{r_1}), \ B := DC.Com_{ck}(b; \mathbf{r_2}), \ \text{and} \ C := B^a \cdot DC.Com_{ck}(0; \mathbf{r_3}).
Witness: a \in \mathbb{Z}_p, \ \mathbf{r_1}, \mathbf{r_3} \in (\mathbb{Z}_p)^k.

• Pick t \leftarrow \mathbb{Z}_q and \mathbf{s}, \mathbf{y} \leftarrow (\mathbb{Z}_q)^k;
• Compute T = B^t \cdot DC.Com_{ck}(0; \mathbf{s}), \ Y = DC.Com_{ck}(t; \mathbf{y});
• Send T, Y to the verifier and set the state \mathbf{st} := (w, t, \mathbf{s}, \mathbf{y}).
• \Sigma.Prv_2(\mathbf{st}, e): after receiving the challenge e \in \{0, 1\}^{\lambda} from the verifier, compute t' = a \cdot e + t, \ \mathbf{s}' = \mathbf{r_3} \cdot e + \mathbf{s} \ \text{and} \ \mathbf{y}' = \mathbf{r_1} \cdot e + \mathbf{y}; \ \text{send the response} \ (t', \mathbf{s}', \mathbf{y}') \ \text{to the verifier.}
• \Sigma.Verify((A, B, C), (T, Y), e, (t', \mathbf{s}', \mathbf{y}')): accept the proof if and only if:

C^e \cdot T = B^{t'} \cdot DC.Com_{ck}(0; \mathbf{s}') \ \text{and} \ A^e \cdot Y = DC.Com_{ck}(t'; \mathbf{y}').
```

Fig. 8: A  $\Sigma$ -protocol for commitments of Beaver triples.

**Theorem 7.** The protocol presented in Fig. 8 is a  $\Sigma$ -protocol for knowledge of  $a, \mathbf{r_1}, \mathbf{r_3}$  such that  $A := \mathsf{Com}_{\mathsf{ck}}(a; \mathbf{r_1})$  and  $C := B^a \cdot \mathsf{Com}_{\mathsf{ck}}(0; \mathbf{r_3})$ .

Proof. For perfect completeness, it is easy to verify that all the verification equations hold when the prover is honest. For special soundness, assume we have two valid transcript  $(T,Y,e_1,t'_1,\mathbf{s}'_1,\mathbf{y}'_1)$  and  $(T,Y,e_2,t'_2,\mathbf{s}'_2,\mathbf{y}'_2)$  for  $e_1 \neq e_2$ . The knowledge extractor computes  $a = \frac{t'_1 - t'_2}{e_1 - e_2}$ ,  $\mathbf{r}_1 = \frac{\mathbf{y}'_1 - \mathbf{y}'_2}{e_1 - e_2}$ , and  $\mathbf{r}_3 = \frac{\mathbf{s}'_1 - \mathbf{s}'_2}{e_1 - e_2}$ . For special honest verifier zero-knowledge, we have to construct a simulator that given any challenge e can output a simulated transcript that is indistinguishable to the real one. On challenge e, the simulator randomly picks  $t' \leftarrow \mathbb{Z}_p$  and  $\mathbf{s}', \mathbf{y}' \leftarrow (\mathbb{Z}_p)^k$  and computes  $T = B^{t'} \cdot \mathsf{Com}_{\mathsf{ck}}(0;\mathbf{s}') \cdot C^{-e}$  and  $Y = \mathsf{Com}_{\mathsf{ck}}(t';\mathbf{y}') \cdot A^{-e}$ . It is straightforward that the simulated  $(T,Y,e,t',\mathbf{s}',\mathbf{y}')$  has identical distribution to the real one.

## F.3 EUC helper $\mathcal{H}$

The EUC helper definition is slightly different from the CVZK helper. Namely, it is an externalized functionality that also interacts with the environment  $\mathcal{Z}$ , which allows  $\mathcal{Z}$  to inform the EUC helper about which parties are corrupted. Otherwise, it is identical to the CVZK helper.

{75}------------------------------------------------

#### <span id="page-75-1"></span>The helper $\mathcal{H}$ functionality

It is parameterized by an adaptive one-way function f and a set S. Initially,  $S = \emptyset$ .

- Upon receiving (sid, Corrupt, tag\*) from the environment  $\mathcal{Z}$ , set  $S = S \cup \{ \text{tag}^* \}$ .
- Upon receiving (sid, Query, tag,  $\beta$ ), if tag  $\in S$ , then it returns a value  $\alpha \in X_{\text{tag}}$  s.t.  $f_{\text{tag}}(\alpha) = \beta$ . Otherwise, it returns  $\bot$ .

Fig. 9: The helper functionality  $\mathcal{H}$ .

#### <span id="page-75-0"></span>F.4 Straight-line simulatable zero-knowledge

To prove security of the construction, the VMPC simulator must be able to simulate several ZK proofs, such as proving the correctness of shared Beaver triples. However, in the EUC security setting, the simulator cannot rewind the adversarial verifier, and thus the underlying ZK proofs must be straight-line simulatable. In Fig. 10, we describe a three-move ZK protocol that is straight-line simulatable (SLZK), when the simulator has access to the helper family described in Fig. 9, that is essentially a simplification of our CVZK compiler, adjusted as a standard ZK proof between a prover and a single (high entropy) verifier, without the use of a coalescence function. Our SLZK protocol is a compiler for any  $\Sigma$ -protocol  $\Sigma.\Pi = (\Sigma.\mathsf{Prv}_1, \Sigma.\mathsf{Prv}_2, \Sigma.\mathsf{Verify})$  for **NP** language  $\mathcal{L}$  and  $(x,w) \in \mathcal{R}_{\mathcal{L}}$ , and makes use of an input-delayed  $\mathsf{InD}.\Sigma$ -protocol  $\mathsf{InD}.\Sigma.\Pi$  $(\mathsf{InD}.\Sigma.\mathsf{Prv}_1,\mathsf{InD}.\Sigma.\mathsf{Prv}_2,\mathsf{InD}.\Sigma.\mathsf{Verify})$  (for the family of languages  $\{\mathcal{L}^*_{\mathsf{tag}_\ell}\}_{\ell\in[n]}$ as defined in Section 4.3) and a publicly samplable adaptive one-way function family  $\mathbf{F} = \{f_{\mathsf{tag}} : X_{\mathsf{tag}} \mapsto Y_{\mathsf{tag}}\}_{\mathsf{tag} \in \{0,1\}^{\lambda}}$ . We make use of fixed member  $f_{\mathsf{tag}}$ of the family **F** and the same helper  $\mathcal{H}_S$  where S contains all the tags of the malicious parties.

Theorem 8. Let  $\mathbf{F} = \{f_{\mathsf{tag}} : X_{\mathsf{tag}} \mapsto Y_{\mathsf{tag}}\}_{\mathsf{tag} \in \{0,1\}^{\lambda/\log^2 \lambda}}$  be a publicly samplable adaptive one-way function family. For every Sigma protocol  $\Sigma.\Pi = (\Sigma.\mathsf{Prv}_1, \Sigma.\mathsf{Prv}_2, \Sigma.\mathsf{Verify})$  for some language  $\mathcal{L} \in \mathbf{NP}$ , every input-delayed  $\Sigma$ -protocol  $\mathsf{InD}.\Sigma.\Pi = (\mathsf{InD}.\Sigma.\mathsf{Prv}_1, \mathsf{InD}.\Sigma.\mathsf{Prv}_2, \mathsf{InD}.\Sigma.\mathsf{Verify})$  for language  $\{\mathcal{L}^*_{\mathsf{tag}_\ell}\}_{\ell \in [n]}$  and every  $\mathsf{tag} \in \{0,1\}^{\lambda}$ , the protocol presented in Fig. 10 is a ZK protocol for  $\mathcal{L}$  with the following property: there is a simulator pair  $(\mathsf{SLZK}.\mathsf{Sim}_1, \mathsf{SLZK}.\mathsf{Sim}_2^{\mathcal{H}_{\mathsf{tag}}})$  s.t. the protocol simulation is straight-line.

*Proof.* Completeness. It follows by inspection. It is easy to see that if the original  $\Sigma$ -protocol  $\Sigma.\Pi$  is perfectly complete, then the compiled SLZK protocol is also perfectly complete.

**Soundness.** The compiled SLZK protocol also preserves the special soundness property of the original  $\Sigma$ -protocol  $\Sigma.\Pi$ . First of all, the prover shows that either the statement  $x \in \mathcal{L}$  or he/she knows  $\alpha$  such that  $f_{\mathsf{tag}_{\ell}}(\alpha) = \beta$ . Since  $\mathbf{F}$  is a publicly samplable adaptive one-way function family, the PPT adversary has negligible probability to find the pre-image  $\alpha$  such that  $f_{\mathsf{tag}}(\alpha) = \beta$ , where  $\beta \leftarrow \mathsf{IM}(\mathsf{tag}, C)$  and  $C \in \{0, 1\}^{\lambda}$  is randomly chosen by the verifier. Therefore,

{76}------------------------------------------------

```
1. \mathsf{SLZK}.\mathsf{Prv}_1(x,w):
    - Run (a, st) \leftarrow \Sigma. \mathsf{Prv}_1(x, w).
    - Pick random R \leftarrow \{0, 1\}^{\lambda}.
    - Run (a^*, st^*) \leftarrow InD.\Sigma.Sim_1(R, size), where size is the circuit size of f_{tag}(\cdot).
    - Output A := (a, a^*) and the state \mathsf{st}_P := (R, \mathsf{st}, \mathsf{st}^*).
2. The verifier generates a random challenge C \leftarrow \{0,1\}^{\lambda}.
3. \mathsf{SLZK}.\mathsf{Prv}_2(x,w,C,\mathsf{st}_P):
    - Parse \operatorname{st}_P = (R, \operatorname{st}, \operatorname{st}^*) and \operatorname{set} E := R \oplus C.
    - Run z \leftarrow \Sigma.Prv<sub>2</sub>(st, E).
    - Run \beta \leftarrow \mathsf{IM}(\mathsf{tag}, C) as in Def. 9 and define the statement as x^* := \beta for \mathcal{L}^*_{\mathsf{tag}}
        as defined in Sec. 4.3, where tag is the verifier's PID.
    - Run z^* \leftarrow InD.\Sigma.Sim_2(st^*, x^*).
     - Output Z := (E, z, z^*).
4. SLZK. Verify(x, A, C, Z):
    - Parse A := (a, a^*) and Z := (E, z, z^*) and compute R := E \oplus C.
    - Output 1 iff (i) \Sigma. Verify(x, a, E, z) = 1 and InD. \Sigma. Verify(x^*, a^*, R, z^*) = 1.
```

Fig. 10: Three round straight-line simulatable ZK compiler.

the prover has to simulate the input-delayed proof for  $\mathcal{L}_{\mathsf{tag}}^*$ . That means the probability that a prover can produce an accepting tuple  $(x^*, a^*, R', z^*)$  where  $R' \neq R$  is negligible. With overwhelming probability, after the prover outputs the first message A, given  $C' \neq C$ , the prover shall output a tuple (x, a, E', z') such that  $E' = R \oplus C \neq E$ . Hence, with two different challenges, we can obtain two accepting tuples (x, a, E, z) and (x, a, E', z') with overwhelming probability. By definition, there exist an extractor  $\Sigma$ . Ext that takes input as (x, a, E, z) and (x, a, E', z'), and outputs the witness w such that  $(x, w) \in \mathcal{R}_{\mathcal{L}}$ .

Straight-line simulatable zero-knowledge. The zero-knowledge property is based on the HVZK property of both the original  $\Sigma$ -protocol  $\Sigma.\Pi$  and the input-delayed InD. $\Sigma.\Pi$ -protocol. The simulator consists of two algorithms (SLZK.Sim<sub>1</sub>, SLZK.Sim<sub>2</sub><sup> $\mathcal{H}_{tag}$ </sup>) as follows.

```
- SLZK.Sim<sub>1</sub>(x):
• Pick random E \leftarrow \{0,1\}^{\lambda}.
• Run (a, E, z) \leftarrow \Sigma.Sim(x, E).
• Run (a^*, \mathsf{st}^*) \leftarrow \mathsf{InD}.\Sigma.\mathsf{Prv}_1(R, size), where size is the circuit size of f_{\mathsf{tag}}(\cdot).
• Output A := (a, a^*) and the state \mathsf{st}_S := ((E, z), \mathsf{st}^*).
- SLZK.Sim<sub>2</sub><sup>\mathcal{H}_{\mathsf{tag}}</sup>(C, \mathsf{st}_S):
- Parse \mathsf{st}_S = ((E, z), \mathsf{st}^*) and \mathsf{set}\ R := E \oplus C.
- Run \beta \leftarrow \mathsf{IM}(\mathsf{tag}, C) and define the statement as x^* := \beta for \mathcal{L}^*_{\mathsf{tag}}.
• Query (f_{\mathsf{tag}}, \beta) to the helper \mathcal{H}_{\mathsf{tag}}, and obtain \alpha.
- Run z^* \leftarrow \mathsf{InD}.\Sigma.\mathsf{Prv}_2(\mathsf{st}^*, x^*, \alpha, R).
- Output Z := (E, z, z^*).
```

Due to the special HVZK property of  $\Sigma.\Pi$ , the simulated tuple (x, a, E, z) has identical distribution as the real one. Similarly, the tuple  $(x^*, a^*, R, z^*)$  is indistinguishable from the simulated one as in the real protocol transcript due to the

{77}------------------------------------------------

HVZK property of the  $InD.\Sigma$ -protocol. Moreover,  $SLZK.Sim_1$  does not require the challenge to produce the first message; therefore, the proposed protocol is straight-line simulatable zero-knowledge.

# F.5 Fully simulatable zero-knowledge in the $\mathcal{H}$ -EUC model

The zero-knowledge functionality. In our construction, we also need the zero-knowledge functionality  $\mathcal{F}_{ZK}^{\mathcal{R}_{\mathcal{L}}}$  as decrypted in Fig. 11. In order to guarantee privacy when there is at least one honest server, for every ZK proof in VMPC, the server needs to prove it to all the rest servers. Note that this is important and should not be confused with verifiability when all the servers are corrupted. This is because in our construction, the CVZK proofs can only be verified at the end of the protocol. It is too late to prevent privacy leakage due to malicious execution. In addition, as the fully simulatable zero-knowledge proofs are between two servers, they are not restricted to 3 moves. There is a prover,  $S_i$ ,  $i \in [k]$ , and several verifiers,  $\{S_j\}_{j\in[k]\setminus\{i\}}$ . The prover wants to convince the verifiers that a statement  $x \in \mathcal{L}$ , i.e. there exists  $(w, x) \in \mathcal{R}_{\mathcal{L}}$ . When there is at least one honest verifier,  $\mathcal{F}_{ZK}^{\mathcal{R}_{\mathcal{L}}}$  returns (VERIFY, sid, 1) if and only if  $x \in \mathcal{L}$ . When all the verifiers are corrupted, the output of  $\mathcal{F}_{ZK}^{\mathcal{R}_{\mathcal{L}}}$  can be decided by the adversary  $\mathcal{A}$ .

# <span id="page-77-0"></span>The zero-knowledge $\mathcal{F}_{\mathsf{ZK}}^{\mathcal{R}_{\mathcal{L}}}$ functionality

The functionality interacts with the prover P, the verifier V, and the adversary  $\mathcal{A}$ . It is parameterized by an  $\mathbf{NP}$  relation  $\mathcal{R}_{\mathcal{L}}$  for an  $\mathbf{NP}$  language  $\mathcal{L}$ .

- Upon receiving (Statement,  $\operatorname{sid}, x$ ) from the verifier V and (Prove,  $\operatorname{sid}, \langle x, w \rangle$ ) the prover P:
  - Send (Leak,  $\operatorname{sid}$ , x) to the adversary A.
  - Set b := 1 if  $(x, w) \in \mathcal{R}_{\mathcal{L}}$ ; otherwise, it sets b := 0.
  - It sends (Verified, sid, b) to V via public delayed output.

Fig. 11: The zero-knowledge functionality  $\mathcal{F}_{\mathsf{ZK}}^{\mathcal{R}_{\mathcal{L}}}$ 

 $\mathcal{H}$ -EUC Realization of  $\mathcal{F}_{\mathsf{ZK}}^{\mathcal{R}_{\mathcal{L}}}$ . To realize  $\mathcal{F}_{\mathsf{ZK}}^{\mathcal{R}_{\mathcal{L}}}$  in the  $\mathcal{H}$ -EUC setting, we need to construct a straight-line extractable and simulatable zero-knowledge with access to the super-polynomial helper  $\mathcal{H}$ . The construction uses the perfectly binding commitment scheme under the adaptive DDH assumption as described in C.2. As depicted in Fig. 12, the prover needs to run the straight-line extractable and simulatable zero-knowledge with each of the verifiers. To enable straight-line extractability, we let the prover and the verifier first do a coin fliping protocol to jointly setup a public key pk. The prover then encrypts its witness w using pk and show that the encrypted w is a valid witness, i.e.,  $(x, w) \in \mathcal{R}_{\mathcal{L}}$  in zero-knowledge using SLZK as constructed in F.4.

{78}------------------------------------------------

<span id="page-78-1"></span>Upon receiving (Statement,sid, x) from the environment Z, the verifier V does:

- Run ck ← CS<sup>F</sup>.Gen(1<sup>λ</sup> ) and pk<sup>1</sup> ← CS<sup>F</sup>.Gen(1<sup>λ</sup> ).
- Compute e ← CS<sup>F</sup>.Comck(pk<sup>1</sup> ; r) with a fresh randomness r.
- Send (ck, e) to the prover P.

Upon receiving (Prove,sid, x, w) from the environment Z, the prover P does:

- Run pk<sup>2</sup> ← CS<sup>F</sup>.Gen(1<sup>λ</sup> ).
- Wait until it receives (ck, e) from the verifier V .
- Send pk<sup>2</sup> to the verifier V .

Upon receiving pk<sup>2</sup> from the prover P, the verifier V does:

- Run pk<sup>2</sup> ← CS<sup>F</sup>.Gen(1<sup>λ</sup> ).
- Wait until it receives (ck, e) from the verifier V .
- Set pk := pk<sup>1</sup> · pk<sup>2</sup> .
- Send pk<sup>1</sup> to the verifier V .

Upon receiving pk<sup>1</sup> from the verifier V , the prover P does:

- Invoke a SLZK with V , letting V to prove there exists r s.t. e = CS<sup>F</sup>.Comck(pk<sup>1</sup> ; r).
- Set pk := pk<sup>1</sup> · pk<sup>2</sup> .
- Compute c ← CS.Compk(w; ρ) with a fresh randomness ρ.
- Send c to V .

Upon receiving c from the prover P, the verifier V does:

– Invoke a SLZK with P, letting P to prove there exists (w, ρ) s.t.

$$c \leftarrow \mathsf{CS}.\mathsf{Com}_{\mathsf{pk}}(w; \rho) \ \land \ (w, x) \in \mathcal{R}_{\mathcal{L}}$$

– If it verifies, return (Verified,sid, 1) to the environment Z; otherwise, return (Verified,sid, 0) to Z.

Fig. 12: Straight-line extractable and simulatable zero-knowledge Π R<sup>L</sup> ZK .

Theorem 9. The protocol Π R<sup>L</sup> ZK as described in Figure [12](#page-78-1) H-EUC-realizes F R<sup>L</sup> ZK against static corruption.

# <span id="page-78-0"></span>F.6 Realization of FV.Offline in the H-EUC model

Many works, e.g., [\[12,](#page-35-4) [57,](#page-37-15) [29,](#page-36-2) [28,](#page-36-3) [4\]](#page-35-3) in the literature provide various MPC realizations of the SPDZ-type of offline functionality. However our FV.Offline functionality is slightly different, as it needs to be crowd verifiable. Moreover, we would like to realized it in the H-EUC model without trusted setups. It is shown in [\[58,](#page-37-19) [18,](#page-36-12) [17\]](#page-36-16) that any well-defined functionality can be realized in the H-EUC model. Such a protocol can guarantee the correctness of the output as long as at 

{79}------------------------------------------------

least one party remain honest. In addition, to achieve crowd verifiability when all the servers are corrupted, we need to transform all the ZK proofs in the MPC protocol to CVZK proofs. In the following, we provide one possible realization of the FV.Offline functionality in the H-EUC model.

Fig. [14](#page-80-0) depicts a more efficient realization of FV.Offline in the H-EUC model. For readability, we assume there exists a protocol that secure realize F<sup>×</sup> in the H-EUC mode; for instance, using Gilboa's multiplication [\[34\]](#page-36-24) as in [\[42\]](#page-37-9) to compute two party private multiplication in the FOT-hybrid world. The twoparty multiplication functionality F<sup>×</sup> is illustrated in Fig. [13.](#page-79-0)

## <span id="page-79-0"></span>The two-party multiplication functionality F<sup>×</sup>

The functionality interacts with two parties P1, P<sup>2</sup> and the adversary A.

– Upon receiving (Multiply,sid, x, F) from P<sup>1</sup> and (Multiply,sid, y, F), it computes z = x × y, where × is the field operation according to F. It then sends (Output,sid, z) to all the parties P<sup>1</sup> and P<sup>2</sup> via private delayed channel.

Fig. 13: The two-party multiplication functionality F<sup>×</sup>

First of all, note that we use the user's coin b` ∈ {0, 1} as the verifier's challenge in the CVZK. Namely, the prover first runs CVZK.Prv<sup>1</sup> in the preparation phase. Then the users post their coins on the Gbb during the input phase. Finally, the prover finishes the proof using CVZK.Prv<sup>2</sup> in the computing phase.

As such, CVZK has delayed confirmation, i.e., the result will be known after the computing phase. It is sufficient for correctness, but it is not enough to achieve the level of privacy we want. This is because we want to guarantee the user's input privacy as far as there is one honest server. However, all the CVZK proofs are not verifiable during the input phase. Any malicious server can cause privacy leakage by deviating from the protocol. Of course, this malicious behavior can be detected in the computing phase from CVZK. However, the user's privacy is already leaked in the input phase.

To address this issue, all the zero-knowledge proofs must be proven to all the servers using either SLZK or FZK. Therefore, if at least one server is honest, the VMPC correctness can be ensured by SLZK or FZK; if all the servers are corrupted, the VMPC correctness can be ensured by CVZK.

In Phase 1, each of the servers S<sup>i</sup> ∈ S generates a partial ElGamal public key pk<sup>i</sup> ← EG.Gen(Paramgp). It then commits pk<sup>i</sup> using a perfectly binding commitment. After posting the commitment of pk<sup>i</sup> on the Gbb, S<sup>i</sup> prove the knowledge of pk<sup>i</sup> using FZK to all the other servers S<sup>j</sup> ∈ S \ {Si}.

In Phase 2, each server S<sup>i</sup> ∈ S posts pk<sup>i</sup> to the Gbb and proves the opening is correct using SLZK. This would allow the simulator to equivocate the opening in our proof.

{80}------------------------------------------------

# <span id="page-80-0"></span>The offline protocol $\Pi_{\text{offline}}^{\mathcal{F}_{\times},\mathcal{F}_{\text{ZK}},\mathcal{G}_{\text{BB}}}$

- Upon receiving (Init, sid,  $n_1, n_2$ ) from the environment, the server  $S_i \in \mathcal{S}$  does the following.

#### Phase 1:

- Run  $\mathsf{ck}_i \leftarrow \mathsf{EG}.\mathsf{Gen}(\mathsf{Param}_\mathsf{gp})$  and  $\mathsf{pk}_i \leftarrow \mathsf{EG}.\mathsf{Gen}(\mathsf{Param}_\mathsf{gp})$ .
- Compute  $e_i \leftarrow \mathsf{EG.Com}_{\mathsf{ck}_i}(\mathsf{pk}_i; r_i)$  with a fresh randomness  $r_i \leftarrow \mathbb{Z}_p$ .
- Post  $e_i$  (i.e. send (WRITE, sid, pid, x)) to  $\mathcal{G}_{BB}$  and prove the knowledge of  $(\mathsf{pk}_i, r_i)$  using  $\mathcal{F}_{\mathsf{ZK}}$ .

#### Phase 2:

• Post  $pk_i$  to  $\mathcal{G}_{BB}$ , and show that there exists  $r_i \in \mathbb{Z}_p$  s.t.  $e_i = \mathsf{EG.Com}_{\mathsf{ck}_i}(\mathsf{pk}_i; r_i)$  using  $\mathsf{SLZK}$ .

#### Phase 3:

- Set  $pk = \prod_{i=1}^k pk_i$ . Compute  $u_i \leftarrow \mathsf{Enc}_{pk}(0;t_i)$  with fresh randomness  $t_i \leftarrow \mathbb{Z}_p$ .
- Post  $u_i$  to  $\mathcal{G}_{BB}$  and show that there exists  $t_i \in \mathbb{Z}_p$  s.t.  $u_i = \mathsf{Enc}_{\mathsf{pk}}(0; t_i)$  using both SLZK and CVZK.

#### Phase 4:

- Set  $u = \mathsf{Enc}_{\mathsf{pk}}(1;0) \cdot \prod_{i=1}^k u_i$ . Define  $\mathsf{ck} := (\mathsf{pk}, u)$ .
- For  $j \in [n_1]$ , pick random  $(r_{i,j}, \rho_{i,j}) \leftarrow (\mathbb{Z}_p)^2$ ; compute and post  $R_{i,j} \leftarrow \mathsf{DC.Com}_{\mathsf{ck}}(r_{i,j}, \rho_{i,j})$  to  $\mathcal{G}_{\mathsf{BB}}$ ; prove the knowledge of  $(r_{i,j}, \rho_{i,j})$  using  $\mathcal{F}_{\mathsf{ZK}}$  and  $\mathsf{CVZK}$ .
- For  $j \in [n_2]$ , pick random  $(a_{i,j}, \alpha_{i,j}, b_{i,j}, \beta_{i,j}) \leftarrow (\mathbb{Z}_p)^4$ ; compute and post  $A_{i,j} \leftarrow \mathsf{DC.Com}_{\mathsf{ck}}(a_{i,j}, \alpha_{i,j})$  and  $B_{i,j} \leftarrow \mathsf{DC.Com}_{\mathsf{ck}}(a_{i,j}, \beta_{i,j})$  to  $\mathcal{G}_{\mathsf{BB}}$ ; prove the knowledge of  $(a_{i,j}, \alpha_{i,j}, b_{i,j}, \beta_{i,j})$  using  $\mathcal{F}_{\mathsf{ZK}}$  and  $\mathsf{CVZK}$ .

#### Phase 5:

- For  $j \in [n_1]$ , set  $R_j = \prod_{i=1}^k R_{i,j}$ .
- For  $j \in [n_2]$ , for all the  $x, y \in [k]$ , do the following:
  - \* If x = y, do the following step locally.
  - \*  $S_x$  and  $S_y$  invoke  $\mathcal{F}_{\times}$  to obtain  $t_{x,y,j}^{(x)}$  (for  $S_x$ ) and  $t_{x,y,j}^{(y)}$  (for  $S_y$ ) s.t.  $t_{x,y,j}^{(x)} + t_{x,y,j}^{(y)} = a_{x,j} \cdot \beta_{y,j}$ .
  - \*  $S_x$  computes and posts  $C_{x,y,j} = B_{y,j}^{a_{x,j}} \cdot \mathsf{DC.Com}_{\mathsf{ck}}(0; t_{x,y,j}^{(x)})$  to  $\mathcal{G}_{\mathsf{BB}}$ . It then show the correctness of  $C_{x,y,j}$  using  $\mathsf{CVZK}$ .
  - \*  $S_y$  computes and posts  $C'_{x,y,j} \leftarrow \mathsf{DC}.\mathsf{Com}_{\mathsf{ck}}(0;t^{(y)}_{x,y,j})$ . It then show that  $C'_{x,y,j}$  is indeed a commitment of 0 using  $\mathsf{CVZK}$ .
- For  $j \in [n_2]$ , set the Beaver triples as  $A_j = \prod_{i=1}^k A_{i,j}$ ,  $B_j = \prod_{i=1}^k B_{i,j}$ , and  $C_j = \prod_{x=1,y=1}^{k,k} (C_{x,y,j} \cdot C'_{x,y,j})$ .

Fig. 14: The offline protocol  $\Pi_{\text{offline}}^{\mathcal{F}_{\times},\mathcal{F}_{\text{ZK}},\mathcal{G}_{\text{BB}}}$  in the  $\{\mathcal{F}_{\times},\mathcal{F}_{\text{ZK}},\mathcal{G}_{\text{BB}}\}$ -hybrid model.

In **Phase 3**, each server  $S_i \in \mathcal{S}$  sets  $\mathsf{pk} = \prod_{i=1}^k \mathsf{pk}_i$  and produces a ciphertext  $u_i \leftarrow \mathsf{Enc}_{\mathsf{pk}}(0;t_i)$ . It then shows the knowledge of  $t_i$  with respect to  $u_i$  using both SLZK and CVZK.

{81}------------------------------------------------

In **Phase 4**, each server  $S_i \in \mathcal{S}$  defines the dual-mode commitment key as  $u = \mathsf{Enc}_{\mathsf{pk}}(1;0) \cdot \prod_{i=1}^k u_i$  and  $\mathsf{ck} := (\mathsf{pk}, u)$ . Note that  $\mathsf{ck}$  is a perfectly binding key. Each server  $S_i \in \mathcal{S}$  then generates commitments  $\{R_{i,j}\}_{j \in [n_1]}, \{A_{i,j}\}_{j \in [n_2]}$  and  $\{B_{i,j}\}_{j \in [n_2]}$ , and proves the correctness of those commitments using  $\mathcal{F}_{\mathsf{ZK}}$  and  $\mathsf{CVZK}$ .

In **Phase 5**, we define  $R_j = \prod_{i=1}^k R_{i,j}$ ,  $j \in [n_1]$ ;  $A_j = \prod_{i=1}^k A_{i,j}$ ,  $j \in [n_2]$  and  $B_j = \prod_{i=1}^k B_{i,j}$ ,  $j \in [n_2]$ . The servers use  $\mathcal{F}_{\times}$  to compute all the cross terms to produce  $C_j$  such that  $(A_j, B_j, C_j)$  are commitments of a Beaver triple.

**Theorem 10.** The protocol  $\Pi_{\text{offline}}^{\mathcal{F}_{\times},\mathcal{F}_{ZK},\mathcal{G}_{BB}}$  described in Fig. 14  $\mathcal{H}$ -EUC realizes  $\mathcal{F}_{\text{V.Offline}}$  in the  $\{\mathcal{F}_{\times},\mathcal{F}_{ZK},\mathcal{G}_{BB}\}$ -hybrid model.

*Proof.* (Sketch) To prove the theorem, we construct a simulator Sim such that no non-uniform PPT environment  $\mathcal{Z}$  can distinguish between (i) the real execution in the  $\{\mathcal{F}_{\times}, \mathcal{F}_{\mathsf{ZK}}, \mathcal{G}_{\mathsf{BB}}\}$ -hybrid world and the corrupted parties are controlled by a dummy adversary  $\mathcal{A}$  who simply forwards messages from/to  $\mathcal{Z}$ , and (ii) the ideal execution where the parties interact with functionality  $\mathcal{F}_{\mathsf{V.Offline}}$  in the  $\mathcal{G}_{\mathsf{BB}}$ -hybrid model and corrupted parties are controlled by the simulator Sim. Moreover, there is a super-polynomial helper  $\mathcal{H}$  that exists in both ideal and real world. Let  $\mathcal{S}^*$  be the set of corrupted MPC servers. We consider the following cases.

Case 1:  $0 \le |\mathcal{S}^*| < k$  (i.e. there is at least one honest server)

**Simulator.** The simulator Sim internally runs  $\mathcal{A}$ , forwarding messages to/from the environment  $\mathcal{Z}$ . The simulator Sim simulates honest server  $S_i \in \mathcal{S} \setminus \mathcal{S}^*$  and functionalities  $\mathcal{F}_{\times}$ ,  $\mathcal{F}_{\mathsf{ZK}}$  and  $\mathcal{G}_{\mathsf{BB}}$ . In addition, the simulator Sim simulates the following interactions with  $\mathcal{A}$ .

- In **Phase 1**, the simulator Sim extracts the committed  $\mathsf{pk}_i$  for all the corrupted servers  $S_i \in \mathcal{S}^*$  by checking the internal state of the simulated  $\mathcal{F}_{\mathsf{ZK}}$ . The simulator sends (INIT,  $\mathsf{sid}, n_1, n_2$ ) to  $\mathcal{F}_{\mathsf{V.Offline}}$  on behave of all the corrupted servers  $S_i \in \mathcal{S}^*$ .
- In **Phase 2**, upon receiving (CK, sid, ck) from  $\mathcal{F}_{V.Offline}$ , the simulator Sim parses  $\mathsf{ck} = (\mathsf{pk}^*, u^*)$ . Define  $\mathsf{pk}_i := \mathsf{pk}^* / \prod_{j \in [k], j \neq i} \mathsf{pk}_j$ . It then posts  $\mathsf{pk}_i$  to  $\mathcal{G}_{BB}$  and simulates the corresponding SLZK proof.
- In **Phase 3**, the simulator Sim acts as one of the honest servers  $S_i \in \mathcal{S} \setminus \mathcal{S}^*$ ; it generates  $u_i = u^*/(\mathsf{Enc}_{\mathsf{pk}}(1;0) \cdot \prod_{j=1, j \neq i}^k u_j)$  and simulates the corresponding SLZK and CVZK proofs.
- In **Phase 4** and **Phase 5**, the simulator receives (RANDSHARE, sid,  $(r_{i,j}, \rho_{i,j})_{j \in [n_1]}$ ) and (TRIPLESHARE, sid,  $(a_{i,j}, \alpha_{i,j}, b_{i,j}, \beta_{i,j}, c_{i,j}, \gamma_{i,j})_{j \in [n_2]}$ ) from  $\mathcal{F}_{\text{V.Offline}}$ . It then fakes the corresponding shares and commitments accordingly.

**Indistinguishability.** It is straightforward, as the SLZK, CVZK proofs are simulatable in  $\mathcal{H}$ -EUC model, and  $\mathcal{F}_{ZK}$  is extractable and simulatable.

Case 2:  $|S^*| = k$ 

{82}------------------------------------------------

Simulator. Trivial case. There is nothing needs to extract, as the servers do not have input. The simulator Sim just runs the corrupted servers internally and submit to FV.Offline accordingly.

Indistinguishability. The indistinguishability in this case is straightforward, as Sim never simulate a single message as all the servers are corrupted. It is easy to see that the view of Z in the ideal execution has identical distribution to the view of of Z in the real execution. ut
